I really do think I’ve been spending far too much time alone.

Dan’s Final Run: Darwin to Adelaide

Freedom as a ruling passion is the rule of law in Australia. This is unlikely to ever change.

— old Australian saying


I used to drive an Hyundai. Now I don’t drive at all. An Adelaidean junior clown cop just impounded my car. So long, Dan. I’ll miss you. We did 74,000 kilometres in our one year together, the equivalent of five times around Australia. You exceeded all expectations and performed more than adequately. And during our last few days together you were not only my car but also my home. I really will miss you. Now I really am homeless, again.

Did you see the way that creepy little clown cop was staking me out before he pulled me over, even while I was checking in to that motel? Actually, he didn’t even pull me over. I pulled into a side street, he stopped behind me and I got out and asked why he was tailing me. And yes, I was somewhat angry. As I’m sure you can appreciate by now, I don’t enjoy being stalked by evil clown cops. He said he was about to stop me because my LED lighting upgrades obscured a small section of Dan’s Australian Capital Territory licence plate. True enough, but it was still legible – but whatever. So then he asks to see my licence, and then seriously asks me if I have “a problem” with the police. They still use that standard corruption-enabling bulwark: If you have a problem with one cop, you have a problem with all cops. Well, if I didn’t have a problem before I do now. Then he asks me if I’ve ever been “in trouble” with the police. What am I ten years old again? Well the answer would have to be Yes – the usual brand of “trouble” incited by the traditional types of sadistic little arseheads like him.

Yeah, I’ll give you some trouble. Keep trying to turn me into your enemy and that’s exactly what I shall become – and everyone will know why. And no, I don’t really have a problem with the police, just with the shit ones like you. You’re the exception. You make all cops look bad.

So wouldn’t you know it? After removing the offending section of LED lighting, two more cop cars show up. Because depending on your “attitude” while dealing with being targeted and harassed by junior evil clown cops – ostensibly for hastily affixed LED lighting upgrades – the South Australian Liberal Party Police will eventually find some way to ruin your life. I passed all the drug and alcohol tests, and the licence checks, but they got me on an ADR 69 vehicle standards violation: custom steering wheel with no airbag. Stupid airbags. All they do is add excess weight to the vehicle, and damage your hearing if they ever do go off. And that’s not just my opinion. A 2001 study conducted by the Monash University Accident Research Centre and the Department of Transport and Regional Services (Effectiveness of ADR 69: A Case-Control Study of Crashed Vehicles Equipped with Airbags) states the following:


Whilst the deploying airbag has been shown to be effective in preventing more serious injury from occurring in frontal crashes, it should be reiterated that the airbag is only truly effective if the driver is also wearing a seat belt at the time of impact. Furthermore the airbag is only effective in a certain percentage of crashes and as such will not generally offer any added protection in the most severe crashes (i.e. Delta-V greater than 65km/h, Fildes et al, 1996).

Okay, so it doesn’t specifically mention anything about hearing loss, and it is a bit dated, but in the unlikely event of a frontal collision, and since I usually try to maintain a speed of over 65km/h at all times, I truly fail to see why I should be forced to suffer the explosive force of an airbag going off right in my face, in addition to the potential deafness that could occur as a result. I’d much prefer to retain the use of my senses during a crash. And in some crashes airbags have been found to cause more injuries than they prevent – a lot like all the evil clown cops.

So since I have neither the money nor the inclination to “repair” the so-called defect, I’ve been forced to abandon Dan. Now there’s a free silver Hyundai hatch parked on Barr-Smith Avenue in Glen Osmond, unlocked and key in the ignition. Mechanically she’s in surprisingly good condition, although there is a slight wobble in the custom steering wheel under brakes. Brake pads look okay. Perhaps warped rotors? Or worn bearings? Anyway, there’s a bottle of bourbon and a bar of soap under the passenger seat. Do with her as you will.


*

Darwin is one of the worst cities in Australia to live in your car. It’s always so hot and humid, even at night. And then there are the mosquitoes. According to Microsoft co-founder and former CEO (and part-time backyard virologist), Bill Gates, mosquitoes kill twice as many people every year than all the car accidents in all the world combined, whether airbags are deployed or not. Moreover, after suffering through both Zika and Chikungunya in Venezuela a few years back, I must say I’m now a lot more afraid of contracting some lethal mosquito-borne neurological disease than I am of being injured in a car accident. Of course, right now most people who die from diseases transmitted by mosquitoes live in Africa. But that could change, I suppose. Mosquitoes do get around and it is getting warmer. So perhaps my growing fear, along with Mr. Gates’ well-established concern, regarding the spread of Culicidaen-carried deadly infections is somewhat justified?

Given this growing fear, you would have thought I’d have found a place to park for the night plagued with as few mosquitoes as possible. But no. I parked on Casuarina Drive by the Nightcliff Foreshore, a lush tropical green space popular with tourists and the homeless alike, despite the numerous mosquitoes. Yet it wasn’t so much their numbers but the persistence and sneakiness of these particular mosquitoes that I found so disturbing. Despite ensuring Dan’s interior was free from all insects, and after running the air conditioner for about an hour or so, there would always be at least one mosquito still buzzing about. They would only come out right at the moment I was about to fall asleep and would always go straight for my face. Clever little buggers. I wonder if they can detect the changes in our breathing or heart rate when we sleep.

Earlier that day, and before settling on the Nightcliff Foreshore, I drove around all over town looking for suitable parking – which you can do in Darwin in about 30 minutes. Darwin really is quite pretty – a lot more so than I was expecting. I chose to park near the foreshore precisely because it is so pretty, and also because there’s police security cameras positioned all throughout the area. This was back when I still kind of trusted the police. I also just really like the name Nightcliff.

About all I knew about Darwin, before arriving, was that it was bombed heavily during World War II by Japanese imperial militarists, that it was destroyed by a devastating cyclone in the 1970s, that it recently sold its port to China for $506 million, and that it’s infested with huge man-eating crocodiles. I didn’t see any crocodiles; nor did I encounter any other dangerous animals, besides the mosquitoes. Darwin is just like any other Australian city in that respect, besides Adelaide. And here I was thinking Darwin would be full of fat beer-swilling yobs riding around on ATVs and wearing those navy blue singlets and those cute little khaki shorty shorts – the ones with extra leg room that allow you to see straight up into the crotch. But Darwin really is much the same as any other city in Australia – except that it’s smaller and there’s less people, and it’s hotter and more humid, and the people are usually nicer and more laid-back, and there’s supposedly more crocodiles and cyclones, and more young people and Aboriginal people. And not just Aboriginal people, but people from all over the world – although not as many as other Australian cities. There’s also a lot more highly inebriated tourists riding around on orange electric scooters – a lot more than you’re likely to find in other Australian cities. Thanks, City of Darwin. $506 million well spent. But at least now all those alcohol-fuelled fatties won’t drop dead from heat exhaustion.

In addition to serving as the central civic point of orange electric scooter utilisation, the Nightcliff Foreshore also provides residents and visitors to the city of Darwin with a wide range of other outdoor sporting and recreational activities – such as walking, jogging, cycling, swimming, etc. For me it provides a nice warm park bench under a big old Banyan tree (Ficus virens) to sit and drink and smoke and think about how shit my life is. I always find it more poignant and productive to do so whilst relaxing amongst lush botanical surroundings, and the foreshore provides just that. It also provides a concise history of the city of Darwin, via the many informative plastic placards strategically placed throughout the space. A little-known fact I learned from one such placard states that at one point during the 19th century Chinese people outnumbered Europeans by 10 to 1. Whereas nowadays the Chinese only outnumber Europeans by a factor of about 1 to 20. Which really isn’t outnumbering at all, is it?

So as the sun sinks serenely through the sultry haze of an orange-tinged horizon into the blue-green satin sheen of a tranquil Timor Sea, I continue to sit and think beneath the big old Banyan tree. And as a swarm of fat happy tourists on orange electric scooters whizzes by, I begin to think about how to defect to China, whilst simultaneously lamenting my present financial ruin and counting what little money I still have left. Not enough to buy some Chinese food and a can of Coke tonight, which I always crave when I’m broke and which always seems to be the case now.

A family of three riding the same orange electric scooter whizzes by – over 65km/h, no helmets, no seat belts or air bags.

And now the mosquitoes come out.


*

If I were a naval commander planning an attack on Darwin, I probably wouldn’t need more than two men (including myself, I suppose), a rubber dingy, a paddle and a shotgun (probably wouldn’t even need the shotgun). Capturing and securing the strategically important Tiwi Islands would be my first objective, which would probably only take about 20 minutes. With that done, I’d leave one man on the island, to build up defensive positions, and then I’d just paddle on in to the harbour under the cover of darkness. Too easy.

Given the heat and the mosquitoes, most sensible people who live in Darwin don’t go outdoors much. And since Darwin has a proportionately higher number of young people, and Aboriginal people, most of the population stay indoors playing video games all day. When they do go out, they’re usually in their cars or on orange electric scooters. At night it’s pretty much the same story, so I wouldn’t expect any resistance once making landfall.

I would land on the beach at Nightcliff, of course. That’s where all the nice new hotels are, and, according to local legend, where most of the U.S. Marines are stationed. There may or may not be a total of three or four U.S. Marines in Darwin at any given moment to the next, so it is said. To those who give credence to such legends, there is truly nothing to fear. If these so-called Marines do exist, they too probably spend most of their time indoors playing video games, of one form or another. The same may also be said for any Chinese Communist Party spies that may be hovering about, unless they’re at the casino.

After deflating and stashing my dingy, I would ascend the cliff-faced staircase to the foreshore, then I’d check-in to one of those nice new hotels on Casuarina Drive. I would naturally request a penthouse suite with a balcony, which should afford a full view of the harbour – provided there are no catastrophic bush fires burning out of control in the region at the time. Here I would establish my forward command post.

Having thus successfully taken the city of Darwin, and having appointed myself its provisional mayor for life, I would then immediately set about researching the local political scene. Nothing much of a political nature tends to happen in Darwin, unless it involves imaginary cocaine and orgies, so I’d probably just end up going to bed early after a few cones.

I suppose the leasing of Darwin’s port is still somewhat of a political issue, even though that happened a few years back. But I prefer to consider that more of an irrationally politicised shady business deal. And from what I’ve been able to reasonably determine, most people in Darwin see it that way too.


But by leasing the commercial port in Darwin, even though it is well down the harbour, to someone who runs the risk of being a potential adversary over the next 99 years, it’s the equivalent of leasing the Port to the Japanese in 1938.

— Some dude on Sky “News”


I don’t know, 99 years is an awful long time these days. It’s only been 75 years since the end of Word War II. And isn’t Japan now our ally?

The real issue is the leasing of critical infrastructure to any foreign interests, not just to China. Money-grubbing sell outs. This only became an issue due to all the anti-China paranoia whipped up by the media. If the Dutch or the Portuguese leased the port under similar terms no one would have cared. More important is how this deal was brokered.


The NT hired ‘boutique’ Melbourne financial firm Flagstaff Partners to help choose the winning bidder, and ended up paying about $13 million out of $27 million total in fees charged to taxpayers to broker the deal...Instead of using one of the multinational advisers like KPMG or Price Waterhouse Coopers [like that would have helped] for a project of this significance, the NT government hired a tiny Darwin-based firm called Merit Partners.

— abc.net.au


This kind of thing happens all the time under all governments: dodgy deals – even when they’re brokered by multinational advisers. Not that it matters much. I don’t suppose China will be investing in anything in Australia within the next 99 years now anyway. And Xi Jinping has since crowned himself Supreme Militarist Overlord of all of China for life (and all Chinese people regardless of where they live), so it looks like we’re on the fast track to another world war. I say we just hand over all of Darwin to the U.S. Marine Corp. and their faceporn-order child brides and we all mass migrate to Antarctica.


*

I got bored and started to overheat while walking around the centre of Darwin – which you can do in about 30 minutes – and there’s no way I’ll ever get on one of those orange electric scooters, so I caught the ferry over to Melville Island (named after Robert Dundas, the Second Viscount of Melville and first Lord of the Admiralty, not Herman Melville). I went to go see the flame-backed fiddler crabs (Tubuca flammula).

Flame-backed fiddler crabs are truly fascinating animals, with their oversized sexually dimorphic claws and those weird periscope eyes. They have poor long-distance vision but can see polarised light. The oversized claw is too large for feeding; they use it both as a weapon and to attract mates. It is highly visible under polarised light. Fiddler crabs are also good at spotting birds – which like to eat fiddler crabs – because they can see polarised light. Fiddler crabs communicate by waving their oversized claws about in precise rhythmic sequences, and via other subtle chelaen gestures. The way they move their claws about explains why they’re called fiddler crabs: it looks as though they’re playing their oversized claw like a fiddle. They’re also quite tasty to eat, particularly their oversized sexually dimorphic claws.

Sadly, I didn’t get to see any fiddler crabs on Melville Island (probably all extinct in the wild now). But I did get to see a rufous owl (Ninox rufa). Rufous owls are also quite fascinating animals. They don’t do much. They just sit there and stare at you with those big yellow owl eyes of theirs and blink occasionally. Sometimes they’ll swivel their heads to take a look at something that attracts their attention, like the occasional mouse or something, but that’s about it. Unlike fiddler crabs, rufous owls cannot see polarised light. They do, however, have excellent long-distance vision and can see in the dark a lot better than most other animals. I suppose that’s why owls are traditionally associated with wisdom. Like fiddler crabs, rufous owls are sexually dimporhic. Males are larger and heavier than females, which is unusual for owls. Rufus owls do not prey on fiddler crabs. However, they do prey on just about everything else. That’s probably one of the reasons why they’re not at too much risk of going extinct, just yet. Still, it is true that the rufous owl global population size has still yet to be fully determined. Yet it’s estimated they’ve experienced a less than 30% decline over the last ten years, which is the main reason why they’re at least concern of going extinct. Less than 30% still sounds like a lot, especially considering no one knows exactly how many there are. But this is Australia, where most native fauna is already extinct or soon about to become so, so I guess that kind of makes sense. Fortunately, rufous owls are also found on the island of New Guinea (the world’s largest exporter of tropical wood), just across the Torres Strait, which is not part of Australia. But as I understand it, most of the wood is exported to China to make cheap mass-produced guitars. So I guess we can’t complain about that too much, in that case.

The Melville Island rufous owl I met (I shall call her Rufus) was introduced to me by a rather portly young “Aussie” lass I met at the ferry terminal. I shall call her Annabel. Annabel was dressed all in black and had long flaming red hair. She told me she worked at the Cheesecake Shop and had just finished her shift. I didn’t believe her of course. It was only 12pm. But she did talk her mother into allowing me to come back to her place for a beer, once it was determined everything else was closed, that there was no other transportation and that the next ferry back to the mainland wasn’t for another few hours – and once I had became aware of the sad fact that there simply were no fiddler crabs to be seen on Melville Island. Annabel’s mother (I shall call her Minnie) was a rather portly middle-aged “Aussie” lass with long silver hair and a stern care-worn face. She wore a loose-fitting white K-mart summer flower dress and drove an early model Subaru Forester. Her standoffish yet reluctantly hospitable demeanour was somewhat off-putting, I must admit. But I tried as best I could to overlook all that and pretend I was not so ill at ease. People go missing all the time in the “Top End” and fresh meat can become quite hard to come by. They asked me where I had come from and what I was doing in Darwin, and why I had come to Melville Island. I said nothing of the fiddler crabs, primarily because I didn’t think they would have believed me if I had. I instead focused on the first part of the question. I told them I had fled Canberra after discovering how insanely corrupt and incompetent the government there was at the time, and how I decided to drive around Australia in my sturdy little Hyundai, funded entirely by my $10,000 forced early retirement Covid-19 “relief” payment. They both seemed totally understanding and entirely satisfied with that explanation. In fact, I detected in Minnie, in particular, a kindred spirit of sorts. She too, or so it did seem to me, although she spoke not a word of it, had recently experienced a traumatic setback in life. I intuitively associated this past trauma with the palpably sensed absence of a male presence in the house – an absence that until quite recently had not been so. Indeed, as I entered their house, the very dog hair-matted armchair in which Annabel directed me to be seated confirmed to me – according to my olfactory sense at the time – that I was occupying the very seat of that recently made absent male presence. I call it a house, yet I do so merely in name only. It may more accurately be described as a large furnished tin shed out the stinking hot scrub – no more than a dozen or so roughly hewn wooden beams assembled into the shape of a lopsided three-dimensional rectangle held together loosely by a mottled assortment of rusty corrugated iron panels. It was swelteringly hot and humid inside. There was no air-conditioning. But there was a television (on mute) and Rufus the owl. An episode of King of Queens was playing on the television as I assumed the distinguished honour of the lately vacated canine moult-adorned throne, while Annabel proceeded to introduce me to Rufus the owl. There was no beer. I was offered a can of 7 Up instead. Rufus stared at me with her big yellow owl eyes for a few seconds while I sipped my can of 7 Up, then she casually returned her supernatural noctuan gaze to the television. I, on the other hand, could not take my eyes off her. She was truly one of the most fascinating and alluring creatures I have ever beheld – her fine velvety plumage of brown, grey and white cryptically arrayed in multilayered striped patterns; her large and powerful talons; and those huge deep yellow eyes, like two partially eclipsed fiery moons blazing away in the night sky. Rufous owls may have excellent long-distance vision but they have difficulty focusing on objects at shorter distances. I therefore wasn’t too perturbed by Rufus’ obvious lack of interest in my presence. I was, however, quite saddened to learn that Rufus was soon about to die. Minnie informed me that Rufus was a rescue owl. Rufus had suffered an injury to one of her wings and was rescued from certain death by Minnie, only now to be put to death by official order of the Australian Department of Agriculture, Fisheries and Forestry. It is illegal to keep rufous owls without a permit, and obtaining one is no simple matter. Typical Australian federal government bureaucratic bullshit. Bird-murdering fascist scum! I didn’t say that at the time. I think I said something about there being more regulations around killing animals in this country than there are laws protecting and preserving them. I didn’t have much more to say after that, and nor did they (other than casually referring to me as “croc bait”), and I had absolutely no interest in watching King of Queens (muted or otherwise), so I inquired delicately as to the timing of the next ferry. Hint gladly taken, Minnie and Annabel graciously drove me back to the ferry jetty in their early model Subaru Forester. We said our goodbyes and I thanked them both kindly for their obligatory hospitality. It was still another 45 minutes before the ferry was due to arrive. I passed the time by walking around the shoreline looking for any signs of fiddler crabs, while also being as careful as I could not to run into any stray crocs. I encountered neither.

Later that same evening, while walking along the Nightcliff foreshore, I met another rufous owl. She was perched on one of the strategically placed plastic placards. I asked her if she was the same owl. She made no reply. Which placard, you may ask? Yes, it was the same one.


*

However much it may shock and appal you to know, I must admit I am not the model of moral perfection you may all suppose me to be. I killed many animals when I was young – even though they were all invasive species (pigeons and rabbits mostly). I never killed no owl though. I did kill a kookaburra once, however – accidentally – the worst thing I’ve ever done. It was the same night I saw a thylacine (Thylacinus cynocephalus). I was suffering the onset of hypothermia at the time, so I admit I could have been hallucinating. But I’m pretty sure it was a thylacine.

I was camping out in the Victorian Central Highlands in an industrially ruralised region infested with rabbits. Most of the surrounding bushland had been cleared for grazing, except for a winding gully that was still fairly well wooded (and up until quite recently even had a fresh water stream running through it). I’d shot half a dozen or so rabbits that day – still barely a dint in the local population. The area was free from myxomatosis and they all seemed to be in reasonably good health. I used the meat to make stew, and for cat food. My cat, Oogy – all black, exceedingly mischievous and long since diseased (poisoned by my political enemies, poor Oogy) – loved rabbit meat even more than he loved fish. Whenever I returned home from a hunting expedition he would always jump up on my shoulder and nuzzle me affectionately, purring as loud as a full-grown panther. Poor Oogy. May he purr loudly in feline heaven forever. Anyway, rabbits had pretty much taken over the entire area, eroding the banks of the gully with their incessant burrowing and forcing out most of the native wildlife – but for a few kangaroos, cockatoos, kookaburras and magpies. A large feral cat, highly elusive and twice the size of my Oogy, had also staked out a claim in the area. I tracked her for a few hours earlier that day but could never get close enough to take a clear shot. She was always easy enough to track. The birds told me where she was at all times, and her bright ginger tabby coat never afforded too much in the way of camouflage. As such, she was always too wary of staying out in the open for too long. In any case, I just didn’t have the optics nor the firepower to reach her at long distances. I was equipped with a puny Ruger 10/22 carbine fitted with a 3-9 x 32mm scope and I always used subsonic ammunition. I don’t like loud noises and neither do bunny rabbits. They have big ears and quite good hearing. I would always sneak up on them as quietly as I could and try to deliver a clean humane head shot; either that or no shot at all, if I could not be assured of a clean kill – a much less crueller fate than to die slowly in extreme agony due to the effects of myxomatosis, I can assure you. That’s how I justify wasting poor defenceless little bunny rabbits, or at least try to. There is no justification, however, for killing a kookaburra (Dacelo novaeguineae), ever. It is a grave and mortal sin. I am truly damned for all of eternity because of it and there is no absolution. His senseless death, which I caused, however unintentionally, shall forever haunt me.

He landed on a tree branch about 25 metres from where I had setup camp. It was approximately half an hour after sunset. I was about to fall asleep. It was that hour of day when the evocative late-afternoon warbling of magpies begins to fade against a rising chorus of congenial chirps, tweets and bleeps. This tranquil and soothing soundscape lulled me to sleep almost instantly – until, that is, Mister Kookaburra started up. Mister Kookaburra (I shall call him Rex) suddenly let out a piercing burst of raucous laughter that resounded all throughout the gully and silenced every other living creature in the vicinity. It was so loud and jarring I almost jumped out of my skin, as the old expression goes. And not only was Rex’s cachinnating call inordinately loud, but he went on at it for over ten minutes. As much as I love kookaburras in all their regal rambunctious splendour, Rex was really beginning to piss me off. I would have thrown something at him if he were closer – it was too dark to see anything by that time anyway – so I picked up my puny carbine and fired off a round in his general direction. As the crack of the rifle shot died away, I heard a confused flutter of feathers and saw some indistinct movement in the gum tree where Rex was perched – all of which was expected. But I didn’t expect to hear the dull thud of something hitting the ground, nor the heart-rending agonising cry that followed. It sounded more like a human being than a bird. My heart sank, as the old expression goes – like a dead bird falling out of a tree. I hoped in all earnestness I hadn’t killed Rex, that I’d merely knocked him off his perch. But Rex was indeed dead. There was a final fluttering of feathers then all was silent. The entire gully fell silent in fact, as if in shock and dismay at what I had just done. Poor Rex. No longer shall his merry onomatopoeic laughter echo throughout the fairly wooded gully. Silenced by yet another selfish homo sapien and his death-bringing technology. Homo sapien indeed. The knowing ape. A technologically advanced species of ape that only knows how to bring death into the world. As you can imagine, I couldn’t get to sleep after that selfish act of blind mindless aggression, and when I finally did get to sleep I didn’t sleep very well. I tried to console myself by reasoning that Rex must have been suffering from a chronic dysfunction of the hypothalamus or pineal gland that played havoc with his circadian rhythms, causing him to think night was day and that therefore would not have survived out in the wild much longer anyway. But even that entirely plausible rationalisation could not allay my guilt-wracked mind. I then started to think about all those rabbits I’d shot earlier that afternoon. Could I even rationalise or justify that? Could the ecological devastation caused by an invasive species, itself introduced by another invasive species (humankind), ever be mitigated without removing the invasive species that introduced that invasive species in the first place? While pondering that question, I did eventually get to sleep. But I didn’t get much real sleep. My dreams were dark and disturbing, full of vague and shapeless shadows approaching menacingly out of the pitch blackness of the remorseless night. I awoke a few hours later in a state of extreme panic, shivering uncontrollably and frantic with fear. The temperature had dropped to near freezing and I could hear something moving slowly towards me through the undergrowth. I grabbed my red LED flashlight and shone it in the direction of the advancing sound. What I saw, not 15 or so metres before me, I can only describe as a thylacine – because that’s what it was. I know that’s not possible, since the Australian mainland thylacine went extinct over 2,000 years ago, but that is what I saw. At first I thought I was seeing things, or perhaps just seeing a dog. But it didn’t move like a dog. It moved more like a cat stalking its prey. And it was too large to be a fox. Its long snout and broad forehead, together with those large black Anubian eyes, were unmistakably that of a thylacine. The light seemingly had no effect on it. It kept moving towards me, while occasionally raising its head to sniff the air. Then it stopped about five to ten metres in front of me and just stared at me. It was then that I saw two fine points of white light shining out of the bush a few metres behind the animal, like two twinkling stars in the night sky. My hand shook violently as I struggled to keep hold of the flashlight. “Who’s there?” I tried to say. I mouthed the words but no sound came out. Then, from the direction of the points of light, I heard a soft gravelly voice say “You’ve become a little too good of a shot. Now you’re done. Never again.” As suddenly and inexplicably as they had appeared, the two points of white light disappeared. The thylacine raised its head and sniffed the air again. Then it turned side on and began sniffing at something on the ground. At that moment I became absolutely convinced of what I was seeing. It was no dog, and it was no fox. I could now see clearly those distinctive dark stripes across its lower back and that straight thin rigid tail. It paused for a few seconds in that position, raised its head high in the air – almost as if deliberately striking an impressive pose solely for my admiration – then turned and slunk off back into the darkness.

All was silent but for the sound of my heart racing. I’d stopped shivering. I now began to feel hot and flushed. An irresistible urge came over me to get fully naked and go over to where the thylacine had been sniffing around and start burrowing underground. A voice inside my head kept saying do it, do it, over and over again. It was then that I knew my heart would soon stop beating and I would die, if I didn’t build a fire and try to get warm as quickly as possible. So that’s what I did.

I was too afraid of falling asleep again and letting the fire go out, once I got it going and started to warm up, so I sat up most of the night in a state of anxious bewilderment thinking about what I had just experienced. Did I really see and hear what I thought I saw and heard? Or was I hallucinating? That’s all I could think about. I saw nor heard nothing else unusual during the night, but for a few wandering sheep in the distance. I eventually fell asleep again towards morning. I must have slept a good hour or two. When I awoke the fire was almost out and it was becoming light. A thick fog had set in throughout the gully. The ground was covered with a thin crisp layer of frost. I rose and walked over to where I had seen the thylacine sniffing around. There I found a smooth rounded light brown stone, about the size of a dinner plate, with a thin dark stripe running right through the middle of it. I turned it over, half-expecting there to be something under it. There wasn’t. All I saw was damp earth and a few large earthworms. I then went over to the gum tree where Rex was perched the night before. His body was lying two or three metres from the base of the trunk. Rex didn’t look dead. He looked like he was just sleeping. His eyes were closed and his tawny beige-white plumage was perfectly intact. In fact, I could find no trace of a bullet wound whatsoever. I picked up Rex’s cold stiff feathery body and carefully buried it under the striped stone. Sorry, Rex. Never again.

Despite this small yet sincere act of funerary respect and contrition, and my apology, I don’t expect Rex will ever forgive me. I wouldn’t. As stated previously, there is no justification nor absolution for killing a kookaburra, ever.

But I suppose it could be worse. At least I never killed no thylacine.

I shall not digress too far from the main topic of this piece by expatiating too widely on the extinction of the thylacine, nor extinction in general. However, I must make mention of a few salient points that do, in part, relate directly to the general drift of this discussion. To begin with, it must be stated that the significance of my seeing a thylacine that evening has not been lost on me. Indeed, it was perhaps the most epiphanic moment of my life. For since that night I have come to fully understand the true nature of humanity: that humanity itself is an extinction event, in and of itself.

The quaintly naïve Darwinian belief that extinction is an inherent function of evolution – that the demise of a species is wholly attributable to its unfitness or inability to compete with other species and adapt to its surroundings – is no doubt one of the most pernicious fallacies to have ever corrupted human consciousness. As much as I admire and respect Darwin for his prodigious and inestimably valuable contribution to the natural sciences, he should be dug up and eternally burnt at the stake for that one. I need not go into elaborate detail regarding just how much devastating harm that simplistic and spurious supposition has caused, both to humanity itself and every other life form on this planet. All I need do is quote the man himself – and add that no single passage in the scientific literature, written neither before nor since, has been responsible for causing more suffering and death in the world:


As natural selection acts solely by the preservation of profitable modifications, each new form will tend in a fully-stocked country to take the place of, and finally to exterminate, its own less improved parent-form and other less-favoured forms with which it comes into competition. Thus extinction and natural selection go hand in hand. Hence, if we look at each species as descended from some unknown form, both the parent and all the transitional varieties will generally have been exterminated by the very process of the formation and perfection of the new form.

— Charles Darwin, On the Origin of Species, Chapter VI


That extinction is somehow hardcoded into all life intrinsically as the contrapositive counterpart of natural selection (natural deselection, if you will), and that extinction too is a slow and natural process that ensures only the survival of the fittest, runs counter to both experience and considered reason. It is an oversimplified dualistic rationalisation that has given rise to all manner of dangerously flawed misconceptions. As many scholars of the evolutionary sciences have previously pointed out, one need only consider the rather unfortunately named “Great Auk” (Pinguinus impennis), which suddenly went extinct during Darwin’s lifetime. As with the vast majority of species to face extinction during the Holocene, it was homo sapiens who caused the Great Auk’s extermination. They used them to make pillows.

It should come as no surprise, then, to learn that it was that most noble and sagacious of species, homo sapiens, who also caused the extinction of the thylacine, both on mainland Australia and the island of Tasmania. Around the same time Jesus was delivering his sermon on the mount the last mainland thylacine died out; and around the same time John F. Kennedy was assassinated it is thought the very last of the Tasmanian thylacines became extinct. Although it is considered probable thylacine populations on the mainland were already significantly depleted – after tens of thousands of years of human hunting and habitat loss – what finally killed them off was the introduction, about 5,000 years ago, of the common dog (Canis familiaris), which later became known as the “dingo” (Canis familiaris). Thankfully, the dingo never made it to Tasmania; but European homo sapiens, and their dogs, did – about 200 years ago. European homo sapiens also brought with them the gun, and sheep...many sheep. Europeans love their fucking sheep...and their guns.

But then neither are the indigenous homo sapiens of the Australian mainland entirely blameless. They tended their flocks, too, just as European homo sapiens did. Only their flocks were made up of wild kangaroos, not domesticated sheep, and they used fire and dingoes, not helicopters and sheepdogs. Both forced the thylacine to extinction. But then according to Darwin, there’s no need to despair. Extinction is a totally natural process and not at all the cataclysmic tragedy we all may instinctively presume it to be.


...so profound is our ignorance, and so high our presumption, that we marvel when we hear of the extinction of an organic being; and as we do not see the cause, we invoke cataclysms to desolate the world, or invent laws on the duration of the forms of life!

— Charles Darwin, On the Origin of Species, Chapter III


Sorry, Charles, but you were wrong. Current real-world experience, not to mention the fossil record, sufficiently bears this out. Extinction is not an integral part of life. As absurd as it may seem, extinction is anathema to life. Why would life go to all that trouble to exist only to exterminate itself? Life seeks equilibrium, balance, coexistence; and left alone to live and evolve, free from abrupt environmental changes – whether caused by astronomical, geological of anthropogenic forces – life does just that. But then you were a man of your time, Charles, so I guess I can understand, if not forgive, your profound ignorance. You were born into an age when homo sapiens considered themselves the most highly evolved form of life on this planet and therefore had an inherent right and duty to render every other life form extinct; that that was natural. Indeed, many of your contemporaries believed they were not part of the natural world at all. Many of them believed they emanated directly from Divinity itself. Hence, it’s not too difficult to understand how homo sapiens of your time regarded all other life forms merely as a commodity – something to be plundered and destroyed for profit. It is this extraneous and non-biological thought process, antithetical to all life on Earth and motivated by short-term financial gain, that has brought about this current sixth mass extinction event. And that is not natural. It’s about as “natural” as a huge asteroid impacting the Earth.

The tragic tale of the Tasmanian thylacine is the perfect case study. Australian’s (Australian sheep farmers in particular) are fond of claiming that Australia was built on the sheep’s back. That the campaign to eradicate the Tasmanian thylacine, however, was also built on the sheep’s back, they’re not so fond of claiming. Yet it is an indisputable fact that despite accounting for less than 0.5% of all sheep kills, thylacines were ruthlessly hunted to extinction primarily in the name of sheep – at that time the most lucrative source of monetary wealth in Australia.


The climate was too cold and wet for fine wool sheep; sub-alpine grasses were unsuitable fodder, much of the land was uncleared and therefore unproductive; fencing, once built, proved inadequate, as did the inexperienced shepherds, many of whom were convicts; indentured servants disliked the primitive, remote living conditions; sealers, ‘vagabonds’ and Aboriginies helped themselves to sheep, as did wild dogs, devils, wedge-tailed eagles and thylacines.

Records show that many thousands of sheep died from cold and starvation during the first few winters, while records of thylacine predation are minimal...Despite or perhaps because of its being rarely seen by common folk, the animal was feared. Killing a thylacine became something of a rural duty, and also implied considerable bravery.

— David Owen, Thyalcine: The Tragic Tale of the Tasmanian Tiger, pp. 87-90


Moreover, it was widely known at the time that there was a concerted effort to totally eradicate the animal, even though it posed no real threat to livestock. This was no pest control effort. This was a systematic and sadistic campaign of species annihilation.


The thylacine kills sheep, but usually confines its attacks to one at a time, and is therefore by no means so destructive to a flock as the domestic dog become wild, or as the Dingo of Australia, which both commit vast havoc in a single night. High rewards have always, however, been given by sheep owners for their destruction; and, as every available spot of land is now occupied, it is probable that in a very few years this animal, so highly interesting to the zoologist, will become extinct...

— Ronald Gunn, in John West’s The History of Tasmania (1852), pp. 245-246.

The animal is now getting rare in Tasmania. The inhabitants seem to have a superstitious dread of the ‘hyenas’, as they sometimes call it, and will kill the wolf whenever opportunity offers. Indeed, some will even smash the wolf to pulp afterwards, thus depriving science of the skeleton and skin.

— Le Souef, A.S. The Wild Animals of Australasia (1926), p. 319.


The fact alone that the animal was known by so many erroneous names is in itself telling: hyena, lion, wolf, panther, tiger, dingo, etc. There almost seemed to be a deliberate intent to label the animal as anything other than what it was so as to seemingly justify its extermination – in much the same way a human enemy is demonised and misrepresented during an unjustifiable war. Confusion, uncertainty and straight out lies amplifying irrational fears and inspiring violent retributive impulses. But then this was in no way unintentional. This was the law of the land at the time – government policy.


The crucial, final debate on the Bill to eliminate the thylacine from Tasmania – to make it disappear, just as the Aboriginies of the island had been considered valueless and dangerous and therefore extinguishable – is a peculiar reminder that democracy’s strength is also its weakness: it can be so easily abused from within...The thylacine thus fell victim to outrageous statistical exaggeration, blatant untruths and the buying of the racist vote. The power and influence of a political group – wealthy landowners – won the day over reasoned consideration. Facts did not matter; they scarcely entered the debate. During the bounty period, from 1888-1909, payments were made for a total of 2,184 carcasses. That, however, may only have been the tip of the iceberg. Echoing naturalist Geoffrey Smith’s remarks, Guiler noted: ‘Many trappers told me that up to half the thylacines killed were not submitted for bounty but were carted around the local property owners who paid a reward (usually ￡1) and when the carcase became too smelly it was dumped in the bush.’ Furthermore, ‘From 1878 to 1896, 3,482 Tiger skins were despatched from [a tannery] to London where they were made into waistcoats.’

— David Owen, Thyalcine: The Tragic Tale of the Tasmanian Tiger, pp. 101, 105, 115


In 1928 the Tasmanian Advisory Committee for Native Fauna recommended a reserve to protect the few remaining wild thylacines. Nothing happened. Official protection of the species did not arrive until July of 1936. The last known specimen expired in a Hobart zoo two months later. She died overnight of hypothermia after being locked out of her shelter.

Not only did we lose one of the most fascinating examples of convergent evolution since the demise of the Great Auk, we also lost a superbly adapted apex predator we know so little about. So much about the thylacine is still shrouded in myth and legend. We know practically nothing about its behaviour in the wild. And the fact that the animal did not respond well to captivity (they did not breed and spent most of their time pacing back and forth anxiously) meant very little was learned from the last few specimens that died out in zoos. More than that, the thylacine was simply a strikingly beautiful animal. So much lost. And all for a few head of fucking sheep.

As one would expect, the occasional thylacine sighting does still occur, even on mainland Australia. Yet it’s difficult for me to believe that in the age of smartphones and cameras everywhere there is still no conclusive photographic proof of any of these “sightings”. It’s all just guilt denial and tourism marketing, if you ask me. State logos and beer commercials. Ubertus et fidelitus indeed.

And yet I have no doubt about what I saw. It was a thylacine. But was it real? Well, that’s an entirely different question. I don’t think it was real – neither what I saw nor heard. But then it doesn’t really matter if it was real or not. What I saw and heard had the desired effect. I’ve never fired another gun since and never will again.


*

I purchased Dan, a 2010 1400cc two-door silver Hyundai hatch, while living in Canberra, from a high school science teacher who resided in the suburb of Dickson. Dan was advertised as a 1600cc model in excellent condition. But when I showed up to inspect her I discovered she only had a 1400cc engine. She also had a rather disconcerting habit of shaking violently under deceleration. Yet despite all this, I still agreed to purchase her, for $1,500. People who know a lot about cars, and even those who don’t, say you should never buy a car that shakes violently under any circumstances. Being somewhat of an amateur mechanic, however, I was fairly confident I knew the cause of Dan’s problem and could easily repair her. And it turned out I was correct in that assumption. After replacing the engine mounts, Dan was good to go. She already had over 100,000 kilometres on the clock, but there didn’t seem to be much else wrong with her. The only other thing that needed replacing was the sound system. After replacing the sound system – with a Kenwood KMM-105 digital media receiver and four 220W peak-power speakers – the only other thing that needed attending to were Dan’s skinny little wheels, which I replaced with some 15x6 rims fitted with low profile Bridgestone Potenza tyres. The new wheels and tyres improved Dan’s handling tremendously. Still, it still kind of felt like I was driving an old minivan. Hyundais of that era have some of the most uncomfortable seats I’ve ever driven in; so uncomfortable so as to cause severe back pain. I got rid of the original driver’s-side seat and replaced it with a lightweight Saas sports seat. I had to get a steel plate cut to size, drill a few holes in it and get some custom steel braces made to bolt it in place at a lower profile (which took me all day), but once I did all that I was quite happy with the result. No more back pain, and Dan began to feel much less like a old minivan. I also removed the back seat and the passenger-side seat, which reduced the total weight of the vehicle down by approximately a quarter. I cut a piece of foam rubber to size to fill the depression in the floorpan between the missing backseat and the passenger-side front seat. I then purchased a single bed-sized foam mattress. This enabled me to sleep inside Dan quite comfortably, nice and level with my legs fully extended. I also purchased a 12-volt deep cell lithium iron phosphate battery and a 240-volt converter, which I mounted behind the driver’s seat, so I could charge my laptop and other battery operated devices. After all that, the only other thing that needed replacing was Dan’s steering wheel. I imported a hand-made Italian leather racing steering wheel directly from Italy for that purpose. I had to get rid of the driver’s-side airbag to fit it, which reduced the total weight of the vehicle down by approximately another quarter, but once fitted I was more than happy with the result. I also replaced the stock air filter assembly. I mounted a K&N cold air intake in its stead, which probably reduced the total weight down by approximately another quarter, while more importantly also enabling me to hear what Dan’s engine was doing. A few minor modifications to Dan’s fuel injection system were also undertaken, to squeeze a few more kilowatts of power out of her dicky little 1400cc engine. Then the only other thing that required attention were Dan’s headlights. I replaced the original halogen bulbs with some LED bulbs. I then spent half a day polishing up her ten-year-old clouded lenses to near pristine condition. I also fitted some supplemental lighting: a 1.5W Osram Hi-Lux LED light bar. After all these LED lighting upgrades, Dan’s nighttime driving visibility range was extended by over two thirds. Lastly, I also had the rear and side windows treated with thermal insulating and UV blocking window tinting film. Dan was now finally ready for our outback adventure.

In total, I probably spent over twice what I paid for Dan to get her into an appropriate state of safety, comfort and drivability. Once that was achieved, however, I no longer felt nearly so anxious nor ashamed about driving a dicky little 1400cc Hyundai.

Of course, I would have preferred a Tesla. But only rich people who work in the fossil fuel industry can afford one of those.


*

Most of my money over the last few days has gone to Shell, Mobil, BP, Caltex and McDonald’s. I just spent my last $69 on a tobacco. Yeah, fuck it. I haven’t been eating much lately (besides McDonald’s), but I never seem to be hungry anyway. I’ve been living mostly on turkey breast salad sandwiches and Queensland bananas – plus the occasional McDonald’s (usually the only place open). I picked up the bananas from a fruit stall by the side of the road back in Cairns. They are truly the biggest bananas I have ever beheld, or eaten – and the most delicious. The scent of banana tree is so intense when peeling them, like walking through a field of blasted banana trees after a devastating cyclone, and so much more bananary than the ones you buy in the supermarket, which have very little smell or taste by comparison. I should have bought a whole crate of them. But I’m never hungry now anyway. I’ve lost so much weight. I may need to buy a belt soon. Maybe it’s true what they say about extreme anger (fury) being an appetite suppressant? Or maybe it’s all those bottles of triple shot cow milk coffees I’ve been drinking? I suppose that would also explain the excess flatulence.

Fuck it’s hot here. Why do people choose to live up here? High humidity (70+%) and anything over 40 degrees can kill you in around three hours, especially if you’re morbidly obese (as most of them are). I’m so thankful Dan’s air conditioning still works and that I’m not fat and don’t have Covid, otherwise I’d be in some serious trouble. I can well imagine what would happen to me if all that were not so: rapid pulse, lack of concentration, unable to sweat, faintness, then coma and death – or at least irreversible organ damage, no doubt compounded by Covid. Fuck it’s hot here. Too hot. My ill-adapted Canberran sweat pores cannot tolerate this any longer. I’m heading south.


*

Dan began to overheat a little on our way down to Adelaide. It was over 40 degrees, the air conditioning was on full blast and we were doing 140km/h most of the way. So I slowed down to around 130km/h, opened all the windows and turned off the air conditioning. As Dan and I cruised on down the Stuart highway, and just after passing through Adelaide River – with the wind roaring through Dan’s sparse cavernous interior and her 220W peak-power speakers blasting out Hank William’s I’m Not Coming Home Anymore – I began to think back fondly on all I had experienced during my short stay in Darwin. The day before I left I asked an Aboriginal woman I met outside Woolworths, named Jin, where I could purchase some weed. After buying her a whole roast chicken and a two-litre bottle of orange drink, and once it had been firmly established that the word weed was in no way a code for sex, she lead me to a block of housing commission flats on the outskirts of town. Here I was introduced to another Aboriginal woman, also named Jin, who supplied me with a fresh “quarter” of Adelaide’s finest for $130. After sharing a cone with her and a few of her friends, and after thanking her kindly, I took a pleasant stroll back to where Dan was parked by the Nightcliff foreshore. While walking along the foreshore, a beautifully restored P51 Mustang with the words “Cripes A’Mighty” painted large on its engine cowling flew low over the city. I wished I had one of those and could fly around wherever and whenever I pleased – the ultimate form of freedom.

Incidentally, my two most favourite words in the Australian English language is cripes. I’m sure you all know why.

Note: Tom Hanks owns a P51 Mustang.

As we were nearing Katherine, and as Hank Williams was dolefully yodelling out his Long Gone Lonesome Blues, I then thought back on the drive from Mount Isa to Katherine Dan and I had undertaken a few days earlier. Considering the conditions, Dan truly performed more than adequately. To this day I still marvel at how we ever made it out of there alive. The road doesn’t appear on any mapping apps. It runs south of Tarrabool Lake and joins up with the Stuart highway. I don’t know why I decided to take it. I guess I just thought it might be quicker than following Highway 66 all the way down to Warumungu. It was a dirt road, but it seemed in pretty good condition – until we got about five kilometres in. The floods had washed away most of the fine gravel, exposing a hard and bumpy striated surface, which meant keeping the speed down to under 25km/h or else the vibrations became unbearable and it was impossible to steer. There were also many large potholes and entire sections of the road that had collapsed. More than once I had to stop and get out to plan a navigable way around all the damaged sections. On top of all that, there were quite a few creek crossings where the water level had yet to recede enough to make visible the rudimentary concrete bridges spanning them. We passed more than a few wrecked four wheel drives by the sides of these so-called bridges – past victims of ill-fated crossings, no doubt. Yet Dan overcame all these dangers and suffered the punishing conditions without missing a beat. You really did perform more than adequately, Dan. I really will miss you.

I picked up some McDonald’s for dinner in Katherine. Yes, it was the only place open. While scoffing down a triple cheeseburger as quickly as I could, I recalled how during my last visit to Katherine I spent a very uncomfortable night by the side of the highway, plagued by mosquitoes and suffering through 30+ degree heat and 70+% humidity. It was about 10PM by the time I arrived that evening. Before rolling out Dan’s foam mattress and going to sleep, I remembered taking a quick read of the news of the day on my personal surveillance device. I then recalled becoming slightly annoyed (as if all the mosquitoes, 30+ degree heat and 70+% humidity weren’t enough) after reading an article written by one of my favourite Australian journalists who, I thought, was applauding Prime Lump’s Covid response a little too prematurely. But I didn’t bother leaving a comment. I never do. Now, whilst simultaneously steering Dan with my knees and stuffing my face with as many fries as possible, while intermittently wiping the excess salt off my greasy fingers, and as Hank Williams started in on Cold, Cold Heart, I started to think about how poor, isolated and truly alone I am now. But then I consoled myself with the knowledge that it’s so much safer to live alone in your car these days anyway – the second-most ultimate form of freedom.

Just before sunset, I stopped for fuel in a bleak little town called Elliot. There was no fuel. There were only two service stations and they were both shut. I asked an alcohol and glaucoma-afflicted Aboriginal man – the only person in town – where else I could buy fuel. He muttered something about the government taking him away from his mother when he was a baby. He then called me a poofter. I haven’t been called a poofter since I was 14 (to my face). I should have told him I used to work with a mob of “poofters” at an “Abo” Institute in Canberra that supposedly handles that kind of thing, but didn’t. Or maybe I should have just given him a brass truncheon and mugshots of all the staff?

I slept in Dan by the side of the Stuart highway again that night. I was going to try to make it over to the next town and get fuel there, but something happened along the way that made me change my mind. It was dark by that time. The sun had set over the endless expanse of flat orange desert, the sky radiating with broad diffuse bands of brilliant yellows and oranges, becoming deep reds and violets, until all trace of sunlight had vanished and the Milky Way adorning the seamless dark horizon of a pristine black sky was all that remained. I pulled over at a rest stop near Lake Woods to smoke some weed and take a closer look at the sky. It was quite disorienting seeing the Milky Way that bright and in so much detail. I had some serious trouble identifying the usual constellations. Eventually I found Eaglehawk (or Crux, the “Southern Cross”). I then tried to find the great Emu in the Sky. I could only see the top of its head popping up just above the horizon (its head being the Coalsack Nebula). According to Aboriginal people, when the Emu in the Sky’s body can’t be seen it means it has left the waterhole, which all dry up around this time of year. It also means it’s not the right time to collect emu eggs.

There are many Aboriginal stories relating to the Emu in the Sky, this one being my favourite:


The Emu in the Sky

There was once a blind man who lived out in the bush. Being blind he couldn’t hunt, so every day he would send his wife out to collect emu eggs. She would always find many emu eggs and bring them back to him, but he would always complain they were too small and become angry with her. One day she came across the tracks of a very large emu. Thinking that such a large bird would lay very large eggs, she followed the tracks and found it sitting on its nest. “These eggs are certain to please my husband,” she thought. She threw stones at the bird hoping to scare it off. Instead, the bird attacked and killed her.

Meanwhile, the blind man was getting hungry and began to worry about his wife all alone out in the bush. He decided to go and search for her. Unable to see, he used his hands to find his way. As he was going along in this way, he soon felt a strange kind of plant with some berries growing on it. He collected a handful of the berries and ate them. Then all of a sudden he could see again! He was cured of his blindness. Picking up his spear, he went out looking for his wife. He found her tracks and followed them. He found her body by the large emu’s nest. Realising the emu had killed his wife, he speared the emu and sent its spirit up into the Milky Way. And there it remains to this day.


Aboriginal people also believe that the emu could once fly, before having its wings clipped so it could participate in a dance competition, preempting modern palaeognathic evolutionary theory by tens of thousands of years.

Anyhow, as I stood there in the dark by the side of the Stuart highway gazing up at the stars, whilst silently contemplating the absurd incongruity of large yet for the most part unseen flightless birds in the night sky, my train of thought was interrupted and my vision obscured by a so-called road train (big long truck) approaching out of the distance, its vast array of high-powered headlights beaming a glaring phosphorescent path of blinding white light through the desert. So I remounted Dan and we headed off down the highway towards the next town.

Dan and I had travelled about ten kilometres without passing one other vehicle when it happened. A giant red kangaroo (Osphranter rufus) suddenly bounded out from the darkness of the desert right in front us. I’m in no way exaggerating when I say giant. It must have been at least two metres tall. Thankfully, I jammed on the brakes just in time to avoid crashing into it, with less than two metres to spare. The giant red kangaroo proceeded on regardless, leisurely hopping its way across the road, totally oblivious to how close it just came to death. I remember remarking to Dan at the time, “Those LED lighting upgrades just paid for themselves.” There’s no way I would have been able to see that kangaroo and avoid running in to it with Dan’s stock non-LED lighting and those ten-year-old clouded lenses.

Of course, now that I write about it, I’d much prefer it had of been an emu (Dromaius novaehollandiae) and not a kangaroo, so it would have tied in nicely with the Sky Emu thing above. But it wasn’t. It was a kangaroo. Hardly ever see emus nowadays. Probably all extinct in the wild now.


*

Morning broke crisp and clear over the endless expanse of flat orange desert, as it usually does. It’s amazing how much the temperature drops at night just by driving a few hundred kilometres inland. I was awoken by a so-called road train (big long truck) thundering past at around 6AM. I decided to go back to Elliot to get fuel there, since I wasn’t sure if I’d make it to the next town. I didn’t see the alcohol and glaucoma-afflicted Aboriginal man again, and nothing much else happened during the drive down to Alice Springs, just more endless expanses of flat orange desert. I’d listened to every Hank Williams song ever recorded, at least three or four times, by the time I got to Tennant Creek, so I put some Jimmie Rodgers on instead. Why do I take such perverse pleasure in listening to antiqued culturally irrelevant music many of my more snobbish and bigoted contemporaries say I should hate, you may ask? There are a variety of reasons for this, all of which I shall now attempt to explain. But please do take note that I’m really only interested in Hank Williams and Jimmie Rodgers (with a few notable exceptions), not all country music as a genre-specific personal predilection.

I’ll start with Hank Williams. Hank Williams was simply one of the greatest songwriters who ever lived – despite being barely literate, musically illiterate and a chronic alcoholic. And yet before the age of 29, within the space of about six years, Hank recorded a total of 66 songs (50 of them by himself), 37 of them making the charts. Hank may have been just another dumb drunk singing cowboy, but he certainly knew how to write songs.

I’m So Lonesome I Could Cry (1949) is a perfect example:


Hear that lonesome whippoorwill,
He sounds too blue to fly.
The midnight train is whining low,
I’m so lonesome I could cry.

I’ve never seen a night so long
When time goes crawling by.
The moon just went behind a cloud
To hide its face and cry.

Did you ever see a robin weep,
When leaves begin to die?
That mean he’s lost the will to live,
I’m so lonesome I could cry.

The silence of a falling star
Lights up a purple sky.
And as I wonder where you are
I’m so lonesome I could cry.


So simple yet so perfect. Full of evocative meaning. So syntactically sound and well balanced. I’m not saying it’s the greatest song ever written. People who say things like that, about anything, are always full of shit. But for what it is, it is perfect. What makes it perfect? There’s the timeless subjects of solitude and death, the essential fodder of all great art. There’s the refrain of I’m so lonesome I could cry repeated at the end of the first, third and fourth stanzas, the repetition discreetly broken by the moon going behind a cloud to hide its face and cry in the second. There’s the dramatic midpoint interrogative pivot on the “cutting word” die, with the next line ending on the word live. There’s the two birds accompanying and mirroring two astronomical bodies. Indeed, the whole song seems to mirror itself – the sense of hearing in the first and last stanzas, then sight in the second and third, with both senses merging in the final stanza. And then there’s all the internal alliteration, whining low with will to live, and the way the other non-rhyming verses seem to question and answer one another, where you are with behind a cloud, and the way the references to colour do likewise, with the song beginning with a bird sounding too blue to fly yet ending on a silent note of hope as a falling star lights up a purple sky. Then there’s the music itself, a slow and easy waltz rhythm tapped out on muted double bass strings accompanying one of the simplest melodies imaginable: three imploring third half notes in the first and second verses, answered by the same pattern in fourths in the third and fourth verses, then back to the third, before dropping to a perfect fifth on middle C and resolving with a casual return to tonic. And then there’s the steel electric and fiddle solos in between, which seem to mimic in playful mockery the sound of the lonesome whippoorwill and the weeping robin.

How much of I’m So Lonesome I Could Cry Hank wrote himself is debatable. Fred Rose helped out on a lot of Hank’s songs. Yet it is well known that Fred never cared much for this particular song, so it’s more than likely Hank wrote it himself. In any case, it’s difficult to imagine anyone else but a bona fide train wreck of a boozed-up hillbilly like Hank writing something like this. As a piece of writing, it has that rare combination of authentic pathos and facile brevity that usually only comes about through direct lived experience.

Paul Hemphil said it best in Lovesick Blues: The Life of Hank Williams (2005). He compares Hank with Ernest Hemmingway, “two men who were minimalists, masters of understated simplicity”, and quotes Hemmingway himself from his preface to The First Forty-Nine Stories:


In going where you have to go, and doing what you have to do, and seeing what you have to see, you dull and blunt the instrument you write with. But I would rather have it bent and dull and know I had to put it on the grindstone again and hammer it into shape and put a whetstone to it, than to have it bright and shining and nothing to say, or smooth and well-oiled in the closet, but unused.

That should be sufficient to explain my fascination with the music of Hank Williams. But more than that, there’s also the indisputable fact that this current era we are presently living through is beginning to resemble that period in human history when country music, and Hank Williams, first came into being. It almost seems as if we’re turning full circle and going back to where it all began. And in much the same way the death of Hank Williams and the rise of Elvis Presley marked a turning point in the history of popular western music (which Paul Hemphil sums up nicely in the passage I quote below), it would seem reasonable to assume a comparable counterrevolution of similar significance is sure to follow.


An entirely new generation had arrived in America, the baby boomers, kids born at the end of the Second World War, and as they neared their teen years it became evident that country music was their parents’ music, not theirs. Relatively affluent, facing a promising new world free of war and economic depression, they were looking for something fresh that they could call their own. The grandfatherly president, retired general Dwight Eisenhower, wasn’t it, nor was Hank Williams. The dead Hank was to them just another old fart whose time had passed, a drunken hillbilly who whined about “pitchers from life’s other side” and cheating hearts and being so lonesome he could cry. They much preferred this new guy, Elvis, with his pegged trousers and pompadour hairdo and a sneer on his lips and a sensuous wiggle to his hips. The number of full-time country radio stations plummeted, many of the faithful outlets like WCKY and even the Mexican border stations abandoning Hank for Elvis, and almost overnight the glory years of country music in the late forties and early fifties became history. Young Hiram [Hank’s given name at birth] was a child of the Depression and the war years, when everybody was suffering, and his music reflected that.


That a similar cultural shift is currently occurring may just be wishful thinking on my part, I concede. Yet I’m in no way suggesting a literal return to the past. I’m merely hinting at the resurgence of a particular esprit de l’époque that tends to occur during times such as these. What form that takes is of course an entirely different matter. Some would argue that this “shift” has already taken place. Yet I would argue that if it has it’s just the beginning. In any event, I’m sure you all know what I’m getting at. And it could’t happen soon enough, if you ask me. I can’t wait for the day when we finally bury Elvis.

With respect to Jimmie Rodgers, often credited as the father of country music, I need only quote the lyrics of Ben Dewberry’s Final Run (1927) – written by a blind old preacher man named Andrew Jenkins – then recount what I experienced once I arrived at Alice Springs:


Ben Dewberry was a brave engineer
He told his fireman don’t you ever fear
All I want is the water and coal
Put your head out the window, watch the drivers roll
Watch the drivers roll, watch the drivers roll
Put your head out the window, watch the drivers roll

Ben Dewberry said before he died
Two more roads that he wanted to ride
His fireman asked him what could they be
Said the old Northeastern and the A and B
The A and B. He said the A and B
It’s the old Northeastern and the A and B

On the fatal morning it began to rain
Around the curve come a passenger train
Ben Dewberry was the engineer
With the throttle wide open and without any fear
He didn’t have no fear. He didn’t have no fear
He had her runnin’ wide open without any fear

Ben looked at his watch...shook his head
We may make Atlanta but we’ll all be dead
The train was flyin’ by the troublin’ switch
Without any warning then she took the ditch
Yea, she went in the ditch. Well, she took the ditch
Without any warning then she took the ditch

The big locomotive leaped from the rail
Ben never lived to tell that awful tale
His life was ended and his work was done
When Ben Dewberry made his final run
He made his final run. He made his final run
When Ben Dewberry made his final run


Unlike Hank Williams, Jimmie Rodgers did not write most of his own material. When he wasn’t stealing songs from blind old preacher men, Jimmie got a very talented and kindhearted woman named Elsie McWilliams to write most of them for him (for free). Nevertheless, Jimmie certainly knew how to sing them a lot better than most, especially when he was singing about trains. Jimmie once worked as a brakeman, until the introduction of the fail-safe air braking system, which replaced the need for brakeman. I guess that’s part of the reason why. In addition to that, he was living through Prohibition and the Great Depression when he recorded most of the songs he stole, while also suffering from tuberculous and knowing he didn’t have very long to live – which you can kind of hear in his voice.

I’d listened to every Jimmie Rodgers song ever recorded by the time I got to Alice Springs, more than a few times. Ben Dewberry’s Final Run was playing when I finally arrived. In case you didn’t know, the song is about Benjamin Franklin Dewberry (31 Aug 1857 – 23 Aug 1908) from Gwinnett County, Georgia. He was an engineer on the Southern Railway’s Number 38. Ben was killed when the Number 38 derailed and crashed. Some teenage boys placed a bolt on the tracks. They “wanted to see what a wreck would look like”. Whether all that’s true or not, or whether Ben was just going too fast, I cannot say. All I know is it’s a brilliantly sad song – particularly when one considers there were still two more roads Ben wanted to ride – and Jimmie certainly does sing it like he lived it.

The strangest feeling came over me as I drove around town singing along to Ben Dewberry’s Final Run, whilst also marvelling at what an absolute shit hole Alice Springs is. It sounded and felt like Jimmie was right there in the car with me, as if he were sitting right beside me, right there where the passenger seat would have been. I actually turned my head and looked over to see if I could see him sitting there. I couldn’t, of course, but I could keenly sense his presence. And when Jimmie got to the verse Yea, she went in the ditch. Well, she took the ditch it really did sound and feel as though he were physically right there singing those words straight into my ear. His voice was so immediately present, alive and clear. I could not only hear but also feel every vocalised nuance of grief and anguish, as if my whole body were reverberating with a highly amplified series of empathetic harmonic overtones. I was truly overwhelmed – almost to the same degree as I was truly underwhelmed by Alice Springs.

Alice Springs really is an absolute shit hole. Never go there – unless, I suppose, you really want to see that big orange rock out in the desert. I didn’t. But I did stop at Woolworths to stock up on some triple shot cow milk coffee drinks and a few bananas. I also asked the first Aboriginal person I met where to buy some more marijuana. I can’t remember his name, so I’ll call him Nevil. I don’t know why, but I instinctively trusted Nevil. He just seemed like a really good “bloke”. In exchange for a one-litre bottle of Jim Beam and a two-litre bottle of Coke, Nevil took me over to one of his mate’s place, where I picked up another quarter of Adelaide’s finest for $140. Nevil squatted in the space where the passenger seat would have been and held on tight to the dashboard (no seat belt) as we drove on out to his mate’s place, which was about two kilometres out of town. He made my purchase for me and I dropped him off back at the Woolworths parking lot. After bidding farewell to Nevil two Aboriginal teenage boys riding BMX bikes came over to speak with me. I’d noticed them before riding around the parking lot, exchanging winking nods and subtle hand gestures with the private army of Woolworths security guards. Without any introduction, one of them came right up to Dan’s driver’s side window and asked me “Are you crazy?” I didn’t know what to say. I was genuinely taken aback. I sincerely had no idea how to respond to that question, rhetorical though it may have been. Then the other boy came up and said “Nice steering wheel.” Then they rode off together towards the centre of town. It was then that I began to suspect that Nevil was perhaps not such a good bloke.

Again, never go to Alice Springs. Alice Springs is an alcohol-fuelled semi-prohibition-era failed police-security vigilante city-state constantly on the verge of erupting into violent drunken street riots. But I guess I can understand why. I think if I were forced to live out there in the middle of nowhere with nothing but flat orange desert, no work and nothing to do, I’d probably end up becoming a violent alcoholic too.

Jimmie Rodgers, too, was more than likely an alcoholic (by today’s definition, anyone who admits to drinking alcohol regularly). You can kind of hear it in his voice. Unlike Hank Williams, however, Jimmie seemed to have exercised a lot more self-control over his drinking. But by today’s definition an alcoholic is an alcoholic – it’s merely a measure of degrees.

But more importantly, were Hank and Jimmie racists? Probably. Most white people hailing from that part of the world at that time were. Yet none of their work suggests as much (spoken word recordings excepted). Indeed, Hank Williams credits an old black man named Tee-Tot with teaching him the blues, and Jimmie Rodgers learned his trade from riding the rails with hobos and railroad workers, most of whom were more than likely black. Jimmie even recorded with Louis Armstrong and his wife Lillian.

But even more importantly, were they misogynists? Probably. But again, in all likelihood no more so than one would ordinarily expect. There are, however, some fairly overt shades of passive misogyny in Hank’s music, and some even less passive and more overt ones in Jimmie’s. And it is true that Hank often traded blows with his wife. He even fired a gun in her general direction once. Jimmie, on the other hand, was a lot less of a drunk and not so much of a womaniser. He was probably too sick with tuberculosis for that kind of thing anyway.

Ultimately, Hank Williams and Jimmie Rodgers were both men of their place and time. We can really only judge them by their work – and even then with the unfair advantage of considerable hindsight. It may be true that no one sympathises much these days with “niggrah”-hating, wife-beating, drunken cowboys forever wallowing in self-pity while crying in their beers over the adverse life circumstances they themselves more than likely caused, but is that any reason to totally dismiss all their music? Do we stop listening to Bach simply because he was a 17th century cross-dressing Jesus Nazi? Regardless of how artists conduct their personal lives or what they believe, I see no sense nor reason in excising whole periods of musical history just because the mores of a particular cultural era offend our highly enlightened modern-day sensibilities – unless they happen to be straight-up fascists...or Tom Hanks.


*

Nothing much happened during the drive from Alice Springs down to Adelaide, apart from yet more endless expanses of flat orange desert. I listened to Bach’s Art of the Fuge most of the way (the Emerson String Quartet 19th-century 2003 recording). I remember there being no Covid checkpoint at the South Australian border. And I remember driving through Coober Pedy and thinking I should stop there to check out some of those underground miner houses. But I didn’t.
