Ugh! I can really feel the difference now after quitting smoking and switching to vaping over a year ago. Very tiring. It’s exhausting, especially with all the smog and all the walking I was doing. I’ve started coughing again more than usual, and I’m getting way too much nicotine, being forced to smoke a whole cigarette at once. I’m so used to taking one or two vape puffs here and there whenever I feel like it. Also, it’s illegal to smoke outside in Japan. But hardly anyone smokes here anymore anyway. It’s a myth. Nor do all Japanese schoolgirls wear sexy sailor suit schoolgirl uniforms – that’s a myth too. Nor do Japanese business men drink excessively and pass out on the street in full business attire – also a myth. And not all Japanese people are into anime. And Japan really isn’t that expensive – biggest myth of them all. It’s about the same price for most things here as everywhere else: standard expensive. A lot of things are actually less expensive here than in New Zealand, particularly tobacco.


*

It’s the 25th of April, 2023. I’m in Hiroshima. It rained all day. I didn’t have an umbrella and I didn’t bother buying one. I woke up early, drank three cups of instant coffee, then walked all the way to the Hiroshima Peace Memorial, in the rain. It was a grey and sombre Sunday morning. No one else was around. But at least it wasn’t cold. It was actually quite warm. It felt more like summer, if not for the constant rain.

It exploded about 600 metres above the ground. The target was the bridge. They missed. They hit the hospital, of course. Military humour. The bomb contained 64 kilograms of highly enriched uranium (only one kilogram achieved fission), with a blast yield equivalent to 15 kilotons of TNT. Approximately 140,000 people were killed (over a third instantly), and over 70% of the city was destroyed. On the plus side, most of the radiation dispersed within days. Today, radiation levels are much the same as trace amounts found anywhere else in the world.

It was a fine clear morning the day they dropped it. They’d been saving something special for Hiroshima. And you know when the military uses the word special they really mean it. The city had been spared the indiscriminate incendiary carpet bombing that had devastated nearly every other inhabited region of Japan. I suppose that makes sense. When you’re testing out a new form of atomic weaponry in the field, you would naturally wish to detonate it over a nice clean target. Only then could you accurately assess how much death and destruction you’ve caused.

They call it the Genbaku Dōmu, or the Atomic Bomb Dome. That it still stands today is a true miracle of structural engineering. The Product Exhibition Hall building (as it was known then) was built to withstand earthquakes, but it was never designed to survive a direct nuclear strike. Most of the core structure did survive, however, including the dome. It’s a grounded hemispherical braced dome, measuring approximately ten metres wide by four metres high. As we all know from our high school physics textbooks, domes of this type generate significant horizontal thrust at their haunches. They achieve something structural engineers call “perfect compression”, an evenly balanced distribution of latitudinal and longitudinal forces.

The dome was covered in copper plate, all of which was blown away by the force of the blast – a force of over 35 tons per square metre, with a wind speed of approximately 440 metres per second. What remains of the dome, essentially the bare steel framework of half a blasted sphere, sits atop a 25-metre-high tower of steel-reinforced concrete. On either side of the tower are the remnants of the adjoining outer walls, and at the front (the side that faces the river) the vestiges of the two supporting walls of what was once the main entrance. The extra support these walls provided – much like that of a reinforced keep or the watchtower of a medieval fortification – and the fact that the blast was for the most part directed downwards, no doubt greatly contributed to the miraculous survival of the Genbaku Dōmu.

As we all know from our high school Latin textbooks, the word dome means “house” (domicile, domestic, etc.). Domes are one of the oldest known structures. The earliest type of constructed shelter was more or less circular in plan and covered by a curved roof, inevitably inspiring symbolic associations with “the dome of heaven” and “the womb of creation”, whilst also representing the strength, balance and unity imparted by the natural synclastic symmetry of the form itself. Much symbolism. You can understand why they decided to leave it standing.

At the time, the building was being used as an air-raid shelter for government officials and employees of the Hiroshima Prefectural Industrial Promotion Bureau. As I walked by the crumbling ruins and stared up at the dome, I began to wonder what it would have been like working at the Hiroshima Prefectural Industrial Promotion Bureau that day. They say everyone in the building was vaporised instantly. Depends what they mean by instantly. The dome was only 150 metres or so from the epicentre, but there still would have been a split second between the flash and the blast. Nevermind how long it lasted, still would have hurt. Besides, there is no time at times like that, time stops.

I tried not to, but as I approached closer to the remains of the building I was still thinking about all those government officials and employees of the Hiroshima Prefectural Industrial Promotion Bureau. I tried not to dwell on the thought of all those people murdered that day. I honestly tried not to regard the Genbaku Dōmu as a war crime scene. I tried to see it as the first work of art created by an atomic explosion. I really did try. But it still felt more like a war crime scene to me.

I wish I could have gone up to the sixth floor to inspect the interior. I wonder what it would have sounded like inside.

The night before, I fell asleep while watching Japanese commercial television. I find it helps me sleep. I awoke at 4AM. Still no vape, so I smoked a cigarette in the bathroom. I blew the smoke directly into the ventilation fan in the ceiling. I could hear all the sounds of the city through it – mostly the streetcars. I imagine they sound exactly the same now as they did back in 1945.

Earlier that day, I took a ride on one of the few surviving 650 series streetcars (circa 1940). I’m still amazed any of them survived at all. Not to get all nostalgic or anything, but they look and sound a lot like trams of the same era in Melbourne. I never really appreciated those old trams when I used to ride them as a kid. I always thought them slow, noisy and uncomfortable. And as a young adult – as a car driver – I regarded them as little more than a dangerous nuisance. Now I can’t get enough of them – Hiroshima’s 650 series streetcars especially so, since most of them were destroyed on the day of the bombing. But this is not just nostalgia. There’s also something very reassuring and comforting about the experience of riding them: all the clickety clackety clanging sounds, the distinctive ding of the bell, the rolling side-to-side motion as they trundle along incontrovertibly on their predetermined rails of medium-tensile steel; and even the occasional sudden jolt when stopping and starting, the sensation of which transmitted all the more directly through one’s bones via the bare wooden seats – provided they haven’t been refitted. Thankfully, the 650 series streetcar I rode that day had been refitted, with well-upholstered seats. And the driver, in her Hiroshima Electric Railway Company-issued peaked cap and pristine white kit gloves, was highly skilled at ensuring a minimum of jolts and side-to-side roll. Just as I was admiring her driving skill, and as I was stepping off after the streetcar had come to a gentle standstill at the stop nearest my hotel, a very sweaty man in a black business suit carrying a brief case ran up to the driver’s window. He looked so confused, overwhelmed and distraught, as if his very life depended on him immediately being at some unknown place he had no idea how to get to. He began rambling and gesticulating wildly at the streetcar driver, who promptly opened the driver’s door and stepped down on to the street to speak with him. I couldn’t hear what she was saying. But whatever it was, her words seemed to have an immediate calming affect on the man. He then hastily boarded the streetcar, the driver returned to her driver’s seat, and the streetcar trundled off leisurely down Senda-dori Avenue. While watching all this take place, I experienced the most intense sense of impersonal déjà vu, as if I were recalling or reliving someone else’s memory from long ago. Eerily, it occurred to me that I could have been witnessing the exact same scene taking place on that fatal day in 1945, moments before the bomb exploded overhead.

Apart from the streetcars, Hiroshima today looks and feels more like a planned American city. But then I suppose that’s exactly what it is. I began to wonder if Hiroshima would look as uninteresting as it does today if it hadn’t have been completely destroyed 78 years ago. But then most cities in Japan have been destroyed by bombing, fire, earthquakes, landslides, tsunamis, typhoons, Godzilla or overdevelopment in the past anyway. At least the streetcars still survive, I thought as I finished my cigarette. Then I drank three cups of instant coffee, watched some more Japanese commercial television and tried to go back to sleep.

After inspecting the Genbaku Dōmu, I visited the Hiroshima Peace Memorial Museum. There were a lot of people waiting in line outside and it looked kind of cramped inside. I woke up early but not early enough to avoid the crowds. A few busloads of busy tourists had just arrived. Thankfully, there weren’t too many foreigners. I could hear some snippets of English in amongst the sea of Japanese voices, and I could overhear a young honeymooning French couple complain about having to wait in line for so long, along with the odd Korean phrase and a smattering of German thrown in, but from what I could tell most of the visitors were Japanese. Yet despite the crowds, and the foreign tourists, and the cramped conditions, I decided to accept the necessary risk and get a quick look at some of the exhibits: big photographs of big explosions and $100,000 infographics on how to make nuclear bombs, for the most part. One of the exhibits was particularly cramped and crowded – the one with the biggest photographs of big explosions I assumed. The young French couple didn’t want to go in. The male component was complaining about the lack of distance. I didn’t want to go in either. Distance was the only thing that saved them. I accidentally elbowed him as I squeezed past them and made my escape. I apologised (in English). The first photograph beside the entrance is the only one you really need to see: Yukiko Fujii, aged ten, the real face of war. “I want people to know that the atomic bomb inflicted continual physical and mental pain,” Fujii said. That part is often missing from the story. Big explosions are more impressive, I guess.

In between watching Japanese commercial television last night, I started reading John Hersey’s Hiroshima (1946), a factual account of six people who survived the bombing and lived through the aftermath. Hiroshima was written in the “New Journalism” style for the The New Yorker by the Chinese-born American progeny of Protestant missionaries. Since he was writing for a largely Conservative Christian American audience, Hersey chose his six subjects carefully: two Christian priests (one Catholic, one Protestant), two doctors (one rich, one poor), and two women (one with children, the other without – both later converted to Christianity by the Catholic priest, a German). Heresy not only narrates what happened to these people on the day of the bombing, but also how they struggled to rebuild their lives over the year that followed. He also wrote a follow-up piece in 1985, Hiroshima: The Aftermath, where he revisits all six hibakusha (atomic bomb survivors) and relates how they coped and recovered during the subsequent 40 years.

Most people tend to think what was once referred to as New Journalism was invented back in the 1960s, back when people still read magazines. Not so. According to those who know better about this kind of thing, this style of writing was invented way back in the mid-1940s by John Hersey when he penned Hiroshima. I suppose that makes sense. It usually takes about 20 years for the advertainment industry to catch up with current trends. For the majority of us who don’t read magazines, it may be helpful to point out that New Journalism (as it is generally characterised) refers to a style of writing where the journalist openly becomes a participant in the reporting, using linguistic and narrative techniques that augment factual detail with personal perspective – basically writing fact as if it were fiction. There is, of course, nothing new about this style of writing, even when Hersey wrote Hiroshima. Due to the traditional demands of straight “objective” industrial-grade reportage, however – where it is expected of the writer to remain invisible, thereby maintaining the illusion of impartiality – whenever this form of “subjective” journalism does make a reappearance it is more often than not thought of as entirely “new”. Yet the “I”, the first person perspective, is still consciously well hidden in Hersey’s narrative (almost non-existent). It is through the experiences and reflections of the six survivors that Hersey achieves a good deal of factual subjective resonance in Hiroshima. For unlike some writers of lesser restraint and discipline, and despite being raised by Christian missionaries and educated at Yale, Hersey thankfully refrains from giving expression to the first person activist voice of the “journalist as missionary”. As incongruous and incomprehensible as this may seem, given the level of human suffering and the enormity of the disaster itself, Hersey’s own voice is as silent as those who suffered most that day – those burned and bloodied half-living skinless “ghosts”, many of whom were to die in agony not long after from radiation poisoning.


The hurt ones were quiet; no one wept, much less screamed in pain; no one complained; none of the many who died did so noisily; not even the children cried; very few people even spoke.

One could argue that this highly restrained, almost numb and insensible, understated and dispassionate tone was adopted merely to spare a preferentially ignorant American public a graphic depiction of the full horror of the tragedy; that in the excess of joy and revelled-in relief of victory after a bloody and costly war, Hersey was simply being cautious not to upset people or emotively politicise what was still too near and not fully understood – not wishing to rain down nuclear fallout on a V-J Day parade, etc. But as Hersey himself remarked 40 years after writing Hiroshima, “the flat style was deliberate”; the avoidance of a sympathetic mediating influence was intended so as to allow the victims to speak for themselves directly to the reader. During the early chapters, one cannot help regard this deliberate flat style as somewhat disproportionate with the import of the topic. The victims themselves seem not to comprehend what has befallen them. They could be describing any other day in their lives – the only differences being the tremendous flash of light in the sky, a powerful blast and the injuries they sustained. They appear entirely ignorant of the full scale of the devastation. But then how could they not? No one before had ever lived through what they just had.

It’s not until about midway through the text that you begin to appreciate why Hersey chose to write Hiroshima in this “flat style”. Only then does it begin to become apparent – through the subtle use of scene, dialogue, perspective and characterisation – that Hersey is in effect writing an American haiku in long-form journalistic form. Hence the perception, back then, that this was all “new”. In much the same way a haiku, due to its brevity, must allow for its subjects to speak for themselves, so too does Hiroshima. Then it dawns on you that this direct yet determinedly understated style of narrative is indeed an act of masterfully restrained tact, which could hardly be more appropriate given the story being told is a Japanese one, written by an American. By the time we’ve been introduced to the six hibakusha, and after gaining some measure of empathetic insight into their experiences, a sustained sense of unaffected tragedy has been established that feels immediately factual.

Nevertheless, and as stated earlier, Hersey’s choice of hibakusha is very much catered to a contemporary American audience. He could have chosen at least one severely injured survivor who died within a few weeks or months from their injuries, or from radiation poisoning (many did). Instead, he chose to go with six people located 1.5 to 2 kilometres from hypocentre of the blast who were not so seriously injured. Moreover, the six people he chose to interview were hardly representative of the majority of the victims, particularly the two men of god (the white man’s god). Yet this choice too, juxtaposed with the two men of science, is carefully calculated and must be considered with respect to Hersey’s intended readership. At the time of publication the American public was still very much entrenched in a racist and bellicose mindset, with the full extent of the atrocities committed by both the Nazis and the Imperial Japanese Forces only still just coming to light. By choosing to include two representatives of the dominant Western faith, Hersey is attempting to assuage and correlate a war-hardened tribalist consciousness by highlighting a common shared theology. The victims of Hiroshima were not merely the expendable heathen hordes – those bloodthirsty wily orientals martially brainwashed into loving their precious Emperor more than life itself; fanatical subhuman automatons willing to sacrifice everything, including the lives of their children, for the glory of Imperial Japan – they were human beings, same as us, and many of them even believed in the same god we believed in. Hiroshima, in fact, had one of the largest Christian populations of any other city of comparable size in Japan. Furthermore, it’s estimated that up to 8% of the victims were dual nationality U.S. citizens. Still, Hersey presented himself with a hard target. After three years of total war against a fully dehumanised enemy, depicting the Japanese people as anything other than less than human and fully deserving of everything they got, even if that did mean utter extirpation with nuclear weapons, was never going to be easy. Conveying the notion that Japanese people could be pious, stoic, humane and contrite – even heroic – was a task of monumental undertaking requiring no less than a forced literary shift of superimposed spiritual consciousness.

That considered, I can almost forgive Hersey for working the Christian angle in Hiroshima. What I cannot forgive, however, is how Hersey inadvertently turned Rev Kiyoshi Tanimoto, the Protestant minister, into a U.S. mainstream media celebrity. Tanimoto was already an honorary U.S. citizen before the war. He studied theology in Atlanta, Georgia in the 1930s. It’s therefore not too surprising to learn that when Hersey made Tanimoto even more famous by writing about his genuinely commendable acts of bravery and compassion in Hiroshima that Tanimoto should then hit the highly lucrative U.S. speaking engagement circuit. Tanimoto even appeared on an episode of This Is Your Life (May 11, 1955), where one of the surprise guests was Captain Robert Lewis, the co-pilot of the plane that dropped the bomb on Hiroshima. Tanimoto shook his hand enthusiastically, with both hands. They even invited two more seriously injured hibakusha to appear on the show (squeezed in between advertisements for Hazel Bishop nail polish) – two of the young women Tanimoto brought with him to the U.S. to undergo facial reconstruction surgery. But it was thought their appearances would be too upsetting for American audiences, so they were kept in silhouette behind a screen.

Hersey attempts to make up for turning Tanimoto into a travelling circus freak show impresario and a “shopper for briefcases” 40 years later in his follow-up piece. He mentions the episode of This Is Your Life, keeping his plainly evident personal disgust hidden behind a screen of impersonal third-person narrative, never using the word I and maintaining the same dispassionate tone and level of distance established in 1946. Yet Hersey’s “I”, of course, is still unmistakably present in how he structures his narrative. The section where Hersey quotes Tanimoto’s obsequious speech to the House Foreign Affairs Committee is a prime example:


“Our Heavenly Father, we thank Thee for the great blessing Thou hast granted America in enabling her to build in this last decade the greatest civilization in human history... We thank Thee, God, that Japan has been permitted to be one of the fortunate recipients of American generosity. We thank Thee that our people have been given the gift of freedom, enabling them to rise from the ashes of ruin and be reborn... God bless all members of this Senate...”

How degrading. I wonder if Tanimoto was on his knees while he spoke those words.

The next paragraph begins with:


The day before the bomb was dropped on Hiroshima, the city, in fear of incendiary raids, had put hundreds of schoolgirls to work helping to tear down houses and clear fire lanes. They were out in the open when the bomb exploded. Few survived.

The few that did survive, horribly scared and disfigured, were quickly converted to Christianity – a fate somewhat more preferable to those who survived the blast but were burned to death by the secondary fires as they lay trapped beneath the rubble of collapsed buildings. Thus, not only were these fortunate Japanese schoolgirls dominated by the destructive superiority of U.S. military technology, but also by the benevolence of American-style Christian charity, which in turn enabled them to travel to the U.S. and enjoy the reconstructive superiority of American-style plastic surgical technology.

Without ever stating it outright, and within the limitations of his own carefully structured narrative, Hersey makes the grotesque absurdity of this twisted narrative plain enough – the cringe-inducing jingoistic farce that is the self-absolving magnanimity of victory at any cost, or of what Robert Jacobs in his 2010 essay, Reconstructing the Perpetrator’s Soul by Reconstructing the Victims Body, refers to as the “triumphalist discourse”:


...the women, who became known as the ‘Hiroshima Maidens’ (Hiroshima otome), fascinated the US mainstream press, who used their journey to obfuscate the nation’s sense of guilt about the bombing of Hiroshima and Nagasaki, by turning the whole event into a triumphant narrative of science and compassion. In this narrative the Japanese are allowed to be present but only in a childlike, dependent and ultimately grateful position. The heroes are the US doctors and philanthropists, who make the decisions, bear the costs and perform the miraculous surgeries, thus restoring life, happiness, and beauty to the Japanese women...This narrative restated the triumphalist discourse of US technological prowess and benevolence that had characterised the initial use of atomic weapons on Japanese civilians...the real story was about US benevolence and technological ability. US doctors could accomplish what Japanese doctors could not, successfully performing the surgeries involved, and restoring the beauty, and therefore the wellbeing of the afflicted women. But who, in the end, was really the more reconstructed of the participants?

— Robert Jacobs, Reconstructing the Perpetrator’s Soul by Reconstructing the Victims Body: The Portrayal of the ‘Hiroshima Maidens’ by the Mainstream Media in the United States (Intersections: Gender and Sexuality in Asia and the Pacific, Issue 24, June 2010)


Yet the fact that Hersey in part enabled this triumphalist discourse, albeit unintentionally, should in no way detract from the importance or merit of Hiroshima overall. Hersey was the first to bring the survivor’s account of the atomic bomb to a Western audience. That he did so less than a year after the event, while at the same time focusing on the experiences of the survivors and staying off the war itself (and the decision to use the bomb), is more than enough to warrant a close reading of Hiroshima. That Hersey’s narrative was subsequently repurposed into post-war propaganda, a “triumphalist discourse”, although contemptible, is something he could not have foreseen. He was, after all, merely a journalist.

The decision to use the atomic bomb on Hiroshima, as we’ve always been taught to believe, was made by Harry S Truman, the 33rd President of the United States. Truman himself blatantly stated as much on numerous occasions, almost as if he were proud of it. He obviously wanted to go down in history as the man who dropped the bomb which won the war. That the war had already been won by the allied soldiers who fought and died in that war, and that the decision to drop the bomb on Hiroshima had already been made by others, are both facts Truman preferred not to acknowledge. Truman was also fond of claiming that his “decision” to use the atomic bomb saved over one million American lives, and perhaps up to seven million Japanese lives. Like most politicians, numbers meant very little to Truman. Over time that American lives saved estimate varied greatly – anywhere from 120,000 to one million, or more. But more often than not Truman stuck to the one million figure. Just who came up with the nice round big number of one million American lives is anyone’s guess. Some say it was General Douglas MacArthur and his ridiculously oversized corncob crack pipe. MacArthur abandoned his troops in the Philippines during the early days of the war and ended up in Adelaide, before deciding to invade Korea a few years later. He then threatened to invade China as well. Fortunately, Truman fired MacArthur before that could happen, yet the myth of one million American lives still persists to this day.

Truman did a few of other good things during his administration, besides getting rid of MacArthur. But sadly, Truman’s insistence on being the man who dropped the bomb tends to overshadow his other more commendable accomplishments. For instance, Truman recognised the State of Israel (against the advice of most of his staff), supported the creation of the United Nations (a good idea at the time), and he officially ended racial segregation (and lynching) in the U.S. military and civil service. That’s about it really. But at least that’s something.

All that wasn’t enough, however, to prevent Truman from becoming one of the least popular presidents in U.S. history – even more so than Richard M. Nixon. At one point, at the end of the Korean War – which almost resulted in the atomic bomb being used again – Truman’s approval rating in the polls dropped to 22%, after an all-time high of 82% at the end of World War II. Being the man who dropped the bomb which won the war only gets you so far in politics, it would seem.

Incidentally, just like Nixon, Truman too was a piano player. (Not many people know that about Nixon either.) Truman played a pretty mean piana, by all accounts. In fact, he was often fond of telling people how his true ambition in life was to become a piano player in a whorehouse. He probably should have. Instead, he went into politics, which Truman regarded as practically the same thing. Not to oversimplify it, but I think this was the true source of all Truman’s problems. But then Franklin Delano Roosevelt was a hard act to follow. No subsequent U.S. President has even come close, and nor did many before him. If only he had have lived a few more months what might have been? Although, it is true FDR perused a policy of unconditional surrender and initiated development of the atomic bomb in the first place, it’s very difficult for me to believe he would have authorised its use on Hiroshima or Nagasaki, or Kyoto.

FDR didn’t like Truman much. He only met with him twice and kept Truman in the dark on much of the war strategy, including the atomic bomb project. Truman didn’t like to read papers anyway, and he didn’t much care for scientists either. It should come as no surprise then that Truman would casually dismiss all the warnings, concerns and requests for detailed consideration presented to him by those who worked on the atomic bomb project as merely “weepy”. He never even read the Szilard Petition, which advised against the use of the bomb on Japan and was signed by 70 scientists who worked on the project. Truman was a simple man who could only think in oversimplified black and white terms. Nor should it surprise us to learn that Truman didn’t much care for journalists. Even many years after the fact he would often become irritated, dismissive and aggressive when pressed by journalists as to the detailed reasoning behind the decision to drop an atomic bomb on two major centres of civilian population where most of the inhabitants were women and children. Fewer than 10 percent of the dead were soldiers.

But to be fair, and despite wishing to own the so-called decision, by his own account Truman was no inhumane and unfeeling atomic bomb-dropping monster. In what appears to be a heart-felt letter to a long-forgotten Georgian Democratic Senator at the time, Richard B. Russell – one of Truman’s anti-desegregation and pro-lynching contemporaries – Truman states the following:


I know that Japan is a terribly cruel and uncivilized nation in warfare but I can’t bring myself to believe that, because they are beasts, we should ourselves act in the same manner. For myself, I certainly regret the necessity of wiping out whole populations because of the “pigheadedness” of the leaders of a nation and, for your information, I am not going to do it unless it is absolutely necessary. It is my opinion that after the Russians enter into war the Japanese will very shortly fold up. My object is to save as many American lives as possible but I also have a humane feeling for the women and children of Japan.

— Harry S Truman to Richard B. Russell, August 9, 1945


Since Truman didn’t like to read papers or consider details, it’s also not surprising his own writing was full of contradictions and self-deceptions.

A voice from above: One who knows not the heart of words can never know the heart of humanity.

Indeed.

He’d already wiped out whole populations, knowing Russia’s entry into the war would force Japan’s surrender. A “humane feeling”, right. Truman could have decided to drop the bomb within six miles of Tokyo on a legitimate military target so that the flash and the mushroom cloud were visible from the centre of Tokyo. He could have gone against the advice of many of his top advisers, as he had on numerous other occasions. But he didn’t. As Truman very well knew would happen, Japan decided to surrender the same day Russia invaded Manchuria – the same day he wrote that letter, and the same day another atomic bomb was dropped on Nagasaki, wiping out yet more whole populations, women and children.

Nonetheless, we cannot hold Truman ultimately responsible for these premeditated acts of mass murder and terror – for what were, and what should always be considered, war crimes. For in truth, Truman merely green-lit a non-decision decision made long before he even knew about the bomb.

Given that Truman was not directly involved in any of preliminary discussions regarding the use of the atomic bomb, it’s understandable how he was superseded when it came to much of the decision making. Another long-forgotten anti-desegregation and pro-lynching contemporary of Truman’s, James F. Byrnes, who, owing to Truman’s deplorable lack of knowledge and experience became the U.S. Secretary of State and Truman’s unofficial “Assistant President”, made the final decision – a decision that was already made. Byrnes was Truman’s personal representative on the Interim Committee, established by the Secretary of War Henry L. Stimson, to make recommendations to the President on how the bomb was to be used. According to the notes from one of their meetings, “Byrnes suggested, and the members of the Interim Committee agreed, the Secretary of War should be advised that ‘the present view of the Committee was that the bomb should be used against Japan as soon as possible; that it be used on a war plant surrounded by workers’ homes without prior warning...’” and that this “might put us in a position to dictate our own terms at the end of the war.” Perhaps not surprisingly, the phrase “war plant surrounded by workers’ homes” is not a very precise one; it may be interpreted in a variety of ways. For instance, a screw factory that makes screws for use in the construction of military aircraft (amongst many other things), located at the centre of a city of 240,000 people, could very well be considered a war plant surrounded by workers’ homes. James B. Conant, a long-forgotten Harvard chemical weapons developer, who also served on the Interim Committee, came up with that phrase. He was probably pro-lynching too.

The senior U.S. military official I referred to earlier while in Kyoto was in fact the Secretary of War, Henry L. Stimson. I’m not sure what his stance on lynching was. In any event, he did save Kyoto from the atomic bomb. And although I’m somewhat reluctant to admit it, I suppose he did the right thing. The casualties would have been much higher in Kyoto. Moreover, Kyoto was, and still is, the cultural capital of Japan (kind of), despite all the present-day six-kilometre-long shopping arcades.

Stimson fought hard to remove Kyoto from the list of targets, which as you’d expect from the U.S. military was right at the top of their list. The destruction of Kyoto would cause the “greatest psychological effect against Japan” because it was such a culturally important city and because there were a lot of university-educated people living there, so they reasoned. It was only after appealing to the fear of Russian political advantage owing to the anger and resentment the Japanese people would inevitably feel over the loss of their cultural capital that Stimson eventually won out. He knew this would have pushed Japan past the point of no return. Other cities could be rebuilt, Kyoto could not.


...the bitterness which would be caused by such a wanton act might make it impossible during the long post-war period to reconcile the Japanese to us in that area rather than to the Russians. It might thus, I pointed out, be the means of preventing what our policy demanded, namely a sympathetic Japan to the United States in case there should be any aggression by Russia in Manchuria.

— Stimson diary, July 24, 1945


Stimson correctly foresaw that the “moral position [of the Untied States] would be weakened in the eyes of the world and in our own eyes” if Kyoto were targeted. That moral weakening happened anyway, and continues to happen to this day, but at least he managed to spare Kyoto. Ironic, is it not, how the lone voice of relative reason in amongst all the mad evil of total war was the Secretary of War? But according to unsubstantiated urban legend, it was the American archaeologist and art historian, Langdon Warner – who also happened to be the real-life inspiration for Indiana Jones – who was the real white saviour here. Apparently it was he who urged Stimson to keep pushing for the removal of Kyoto from the list of targets. How Indiana knew about the atomic bomb to begin with, let alone that Kyoto was on the list of targets, is anyone’s guess. But then I guess everything devolves into a Steven Spielberg “movie” in the end.

Similarly, that Stimson had to resort to selling Hiroshima to Truman as an important military target to spare Kyoto was another unsubstantiated yet necessary reimagining of the truth. It was war time after all. Truman even referred to Hiroshima after the bombing as a “military base”. He probably thought it was. But as another long-forgotten pro-lynching contemporary U.S. Senator, Hiram Warren Johnson, once remarked, “the first casualty when war comes is truth”. Incidentally, Hiram died the same day the bomb was dropped on Hiroshima, from old age.

Like Truman, Stimson too appeared to believe the atomic bomb ended the war. “All the evidence I have seen indicates that the controlling factor in the final Japanese decision to accept our terms of surrender was the atomic bomb,” wrote Stimson in Harper’s Magazine (February, 1947). The “evidence” Stimson had seen came in the form of an article he’d read in The Atlantic (December, 1946), “If the Atomic Bomb Had Not Been Used”, written by Karl T. Compton, an MIT physicist who also served on the Interim Committee. “It was not one atomic bomb, or two, which brought surrender; it was the experience of what an atomic bomb will actually do to a community, plus the dread of many more, that was effective,” declared Compton. Of course, glaringly absent from both Stimson’s and Compton’s assessments, besides the actual “evidence”, is the Japanese perspective. The self-sustaining reverberations that resounded within the echo chambers of the Interim Committee – which was essentially a group think tank – have predominated for the last 70 years and prevented a more broader understanding of all the controlling factors that brought about Japan’s surrender.

Also like Truman, Stimson too believed he was responsible for the decision to drop the bomb. In that same piece he wrote for Harper’s Magazine, Stimson stated that “The ultimate responsibility for the recommendation to the President rested upon me, and I have no desire to veil it.” He then went on to confirm, as Compton also made clear in his article in The Atlantic, that it was a “foregone conclusion” the bomb would be used. It was the recommendation of the Interim Committee that 1. The bomb be used against Japan as soon as possible. 2. That it be used on a city. 3. That no warning be given. That that foregone conclusion originated from James F. Byrnes (the “Assistant President”), as if by covert presidential decree, and then subtlety impressed itself upon the Interim Committee as predetermined policy, is a fact conveniently overlooked in most accounts – certainly in Stimson’s and Compton’s accounts, and in Truman’s most decidedly. Effectively, Stimson merely remade the decision made by Byrnes, and then owned it, which was then reowned by Truman and which was never really a decision to begin with. It’s a classic case of a group think tank feedback loop at work, with Byrnes serving as the main sustaining conduit.

Truman eventually wised up to Byrnes directing policy on the sly from behind the scenes and removed him as Secretary of State. Byrnes later went on to become the Governor of South Carolina. He then became one of the main driving forces behind southern states switching their traditional political party allegiances from Democrat to Republican.

Nevertheless, it must be stated that by singling out the nefarious influence of Byrnes I am in no way absolving Truman or Stimson for condoning the indiscriminate use of a weapon of mass destruction on civilian populations. Nor am I pardoning any of those who served on the Interim Committee. Plainly stated, the use of the atomic bomb on Japan was a war crime perpetrated by all those who sanctioned its use. Thus should it always be remembered. Moreover, it should also be remembered that Truman did threaten the “prompt and utter destruction” of all of Japan, and that Stimson did refer to the atomic bomb as an “eminently suitable weapon”, while both professed a seeming unwillingness to cause unnecessary civilian casualties. Yet neither of them expressed much remorse after achieving exactly that. Ultimately, they were both political men of their time, morally debased by the evil that is total war, who lacked the intellectual honesty to confront their own contradictions and self-deceptions. Thus should they always be remembered – Byrnes especially so.

As too should J. Robert Oppenheimer, the so-called “father of the atomic bomb”. Not that he was really its father. There was no single “father” of the atomic bomb (or mother, for that matter). Thousands of people worked on that project. But I suppose if there has to be a father, Robert F. Christy probably deserves that dubious honour more than most. According to Wikipedia “Christy is generally credited with the insight that a solid sub-critical mass of plutonium could be explosively compressed into supercriticality, a great simplification of earlier concepts of implosion requiring hollow shells.” Oppenheimer didn’t really do much at all to further the development of the atomic bomb itself, other than carrying out your standard run-of-the-mill high-level managerial tasks. In fact, many of his hypotheses were plainly wrong. Moreover, Oppenheimer believed that only 10,000 to 20,000 people would die in the first attack and that the level of radiation would be minimal. But it is true he did possess the highly desirable capacity to inspire other people, and he did present well on a podium. He also served on Stimson’s Interim Committee. Not surprisingly, Oppenheimer agreed with the recommendation that the bomb be used on a Japanese city as soon as possible. He dismissed the idea of a demonstration, claiming that “a test would be unlikely to induce surrender”. He opined that a mere demonstration would not be “sufficiently startling”; that it would be regarded as little more than a “nuclear firecracker”. And yet on another occasion he proudly stated that “the visual effect of the atomic bomb would be tremendous. It would be accompanied by a brilliant luminescence which would rise to a height of 10,000 to 20,000 feet.” Despite being a man of science, Oppenheimer too was clearly full of contradictions and self-deceptions.

In sharp contrast with Truman, Byrnes and Stimson, however, Oppenheimer did have the intellectual honesty to confront his own contradictions and self-deceptions, eventually. Upon learning of the destruction of Hiroshima and Nagasaki, Oppenheimer appeared to express genuine remorse, and he was even quoted on one occasion quietly commenting to himself “Those poor little people.” I’m not sure if Oppenheimer ever read Hersey’s Hiroshima or not, but over the years these expressions of remorse did appear to become somewhat more pronounced. But I doubt this was due to reading Hersey. Personally, I attribute this awakening sense of guilt in Oppenheimer to Isidor Isaac Rabi, Nobel Prize-winning physicist and inventor of the microwave oven. Rabi’s stilted and strained delivery of those two prophetic lines in Atomic Power (1946), a U.S. post-war propaganda effort in cinematic form docudramatising the first test of the atomic bomb (filmed 12 months after the real test), delivered with a wry and intently conspicuous sideways shift of the eyes (to the left), must have continually come back to haunt Oppenheimer: “It’s going to work alright, Robert, and I’m sure we’ll never be sorry for it.” Nothing perturbs the soul like deliberate bad acting. Oppenheimer, conversely, was quite a good actor. His “Now I am Become Death” speech being one of his finest and most memorable performances. With a forlorn and downcast visage and a far-away look in his tear-welled eyes, Oppenheimer recounts in wistful measured tones what he says ran through his mind while witnessing the test. These words are perhaps some of the most overquoted words by any scientist in living memory, so I apologise in advance for quoting them again here. I assure you I do so purely for the purposes of deconstructive analysis, not for mere poetic effect.


We knew the world would not be the same. A few people laughed, a few people cried, most people were silent. I remembered the line from the Hindu scripture, the Bhagavad Gita. Vishnu is trying to persuade the Prince that he should do his duty and to impress him takes on his multi-armed form and says “Now I am become Death, the destroyer of worlds.” I suppose we all thought that, one way or another.


As you’d expect, this poignant little performance is not from the film Atomic Power (1946). Oppenheimer was speaking 20 years after the fact in a 1965 NBC News documentary called The Decision to Drop the Bomb. Oppenheimer had a full 20 years to script that response. But according to others who were with Oppenheimer on the day of the test, his real response was neither poignant nor poetic. He was jubilant and merely remarked “I guess it worked.” To which some Harvard physicist dude responded “Now we’re all sons of bitches.” Real life is not poetry, and rarely are our responses to it ever poignant.

Nevertheless, whether or not this recollection of Hindu scripture occurred to Oppenheimer in 1945 or in much belated retrospect, it is too important a reference not to examine in greater detail; for it is without question quite revealing. Oppenheimer is partially paraphrasing from Chapter 11, verse 32 of the Bhagavad Gita (250 BCE – 500 CE), a 1,400-line self-glorifying love letter from the Hindu deity Vishnu to himself. All the world’s problems would be solved instantly if people would only sit up straight on a yoga mat, cross their legs and eyes intently, say Ôm occasionally and concentrate on the awesomeness of Vishnu for a while, so the copy reads. Granted, the Bhagavad Gita doesn’t translate too well into English (which is probably why Oppenheimer is said to have learned Sanskrit to read it in its original form), but for impressionable young Westerners with no previous exposure to spiritual practises other than Christianity, it can be thought of as quite an illuminating read. In the chapter where Oppenheimer misquotes Vishnu’s “destroyer of worlds” speech (the only chapter worth reading), Vishnu and Prince Arjuna (the archer) are standing between two opposing armies about to go to war. Earlier, in Chapter 1, Arjuna expresses misgivings about participating in the battle. He becomes demoralised by the thought of committing acts of violence on fellow human beings, knowing that only more evil will come of it, and therefore refuses to fight. “I only see omens of misfortune. I do not foresee how any good can come from killing my own kinsmen in this battle,” Arjuna reasons. “Even though they may be aggressors, sin will certainly come upon us if we slay them.” Vishnu, who up until that time has appeared to Arjuna as a 23-armed handsome young man with a healthy bright blue tan and a rather lofty opinion of himself, now takes on an even loftier form: a dreadfully mind-blowing cosmic manifestation of supreme divinity, which involves even more arms – an infinite number of arms in fact – as well as many different types of heads, mouths, stomachs, legs, feet, etc. all blazing away infinitely in an impressive ever-changing array of radiant technicolor pyrotechnics. It is in this most refulgent and omnifarious form that The Supreme Lord thus spake to Prince Arjuna:


I am mighty Time, the source of destruction that comes forth to annihilate the worlds. Even without your participation, the warriors arrayed in the opposing army shall cease to exist.


The next verse (verse 33), which Oppenheimer only contextually infers, provides the context:


Therefore, arise and attain honour! Conquer your foes and enjoy prosperous rulership. These warriors stand already slain by Me, and you will only be an instrument of My work, O expert archer.


This in itself isn’t a very strong argument. It’s the equivalent of saying the sun will die in another four billion years anyway, therefore we may as well kill all life on earth right now as quickly as possible (as we are). In previous verses, Vishnu has repeatedly expounded upon the central message of the Bhagavad Gita: self-realization through the renunciation of the “fruits of actions”; to do our duty and not concern ourselves with the results. The fruits of actions are not for our enjoyment. We must renounce what follows upon completion of the action, not the action itself, etc. But doesn’t Vishnu (in his viśhwarūp or infinite cosmic form) directly contradict himself in verse 33 by exhorting Arjuna to “enjoy prosperous rulership” as the fruits of his actions? Surely this in violation of the fruit renunciation precept. Moreover, if, as Vishnu has also explained in previous chapters, all living beings are an integral part of God and deserving of love and compassion, even the “wicked”, how then can it be deemed acceptable to commit acts of violence on fellow human beings? Even Mohandas K. Gandhi, a life-long adherent of the teachings of the Bhagavad Gita, had a devil of a time comprehending Chapter 11, verse 33:


Let it be granted that, according to the letter of the Gita, it is possible to say that warfare is consistent with the renunciation of fruit. But after forty years’ unremitting endeavor fully to enforce the teaching of the Gita in my own life, I have in all humility felt that perfect renunciation is impossible without perfect observance of ahimsa [nonviolence] in every shape and form.

— Mohandas K. Gandhi, The Message of the Gita (1959)


One could consider Gandhi’s interpretation of the Gita, while laudable, an act of hermeneutical renunciation in itself. I seriously doubt Vishnu would agree with Gandhi’s understanding of “perfect renunciation”. At least Oppenheimer fully embraced the meaning of Chapter 11, verse 33. Oppenheimer did his duty and renounced the fruit of his action, not the action itself.

But then one can fully understand how Oppenheimer would have wanted nothing more than to renounce the fruits of his actions. Who would wish to eat their own poisoned fruit? Who would wish to claim parentage of a weapon of mass destruction which had already killed over 200,000 people and now threatened the extermination of all of humanity?

Like most systems of spiritual belief, Hinduism, as outlined in the Bhagavad Gita, provides a convenient if contradictory caveat for the warrior castes when it comes to the justification of violence, which Oppenheimer only too conveniently made use of (in much the same way the Imperial Japanese Forces made use of Bushido). It is acceptable, indeed required, to kill when duty calls, so long as it is done with solemn dispassion and without expectation of reward. Provided a soldier keeps faith with The Supreme Lord, all sins are forgiven.

For that reason we can’t be too hard on Oppenheimer. As lofty and high-minded as he certainly may have been, in the end Oppenheimer was just another chumped soldier – an expert archer with a nuclear physics degree. Like most scientists, he became yet another appendage of The Supreme Lord: Militarism. And, like most soldiers, he too was used, abused and then betrayed by his own nation. And he did at least try to make up for being the “father of the atomic bomb” after the war. His conscience seemed to have caught up with him by then. After the war, Oppenheimer advocated for nuclear non-proliferation and advised against development of the hydrogen bomb, and he did at times appear to express at least some measure of sincere regret – or if not regret then at least “misgivings”, as Oppenheimer put it. But he sure didn’t display many misgivings during the building of the bomb, and he did nothing to prevent its unnecessary use on Japan.

At the very least, Oppenheimer could have delayed delivery of the bomb until after the war had ended. Just because it can be made doesn’t mean it should be made. The Nazis stopped development of a nuclear weapon in 1942 and it would have only been a matter of weeks before Japan surrendered. They would have surrendered in early August, if not for the “unconditional” condition (which turned out to be not so unconditional in the end). The tide had started to turn after the Battle of Midway, and Japanese territorial expansion was checked once the allies regained Papua and the Solomons. By July 1945, nearly all the territory the Japanese had conquered after 1941 had been retaken. So why drop the bomb? Oppenheimer preferred to believe he was saving one million American lives (most of them Russian). If we choose to overlook that for the militarist propaganda that it is, and if we also discount the most obvious motivation – that of blind revenge – one can only conclude that the atomic bomb was used solely to advance U.S. strategic interests. The bomb didn’t even hasten the end of the war. By late July it was widely known by both sides Japan would be forced to surrender by the end of August, shortly after Russia’s entry into the war.

In Racing the Enemy (2006), historian Tsuyoshi Hasegawa has shown that it was the declaration of war on Japan by Russia on 9 August 1945 that is most directly responsible for Japanese surrender. Don’t worry, I won’t bore you any more than I already have by quoting at length therefrom. Enough to state that not only does Hasegawa investigate the official Japanese war records in support of this conclusion, but in doing so he also prompts us to ask a very important question in response: If the atomic bomb didn’t end the war, then why was it used?

They didn’t just want the surrender of Japan and an end to the war, they wanted a total and unconditional surrender to the U.S. alone. As the operational name they gave to the invasion of the Japanese main islands more than suggests, “Operation Downfall”, they planned for a total downfall of Japan so they could exchange the Emperor for Mickey Mouse and rebuild the Empire in their own image. Just as Byrnes had stated before the bomb was dropped, they wanted to dictate their own terms after the war, unconditionally.

But then the “unconditional” condition turned out to be not so unconditional in the end. There was no need to exchange the Emperor for Mickey Mouse. Hirohito pretty much was Mickey Mouse to begin with – and thus did he remain so, sacred and inviolable, despite rubber stamping into existence a war of Imperialist conquest that caused the deaths of over 33 million people.

If only this more conditional Mickey Mouse form of unconditional surrender had been communicated more effectively to Hirohito and his entourage before the atomic bombs were dropped. But then when you’re operating in a theatre of war where over 33 million deaths have already occurred, what’s another 200,000 or so? Such is the logic of total war.

Regardless, my point is it wasn’t the fear of more atomic bombs that ended the war. Nor was it the fear of “what an atomic bomb will actually do”, as Compton put it. It only became known what atomic bombs “actually do” months later. In any event, the fire bombing of Tokyo caused higher casualties and more widespread destruction. Conventional munitions, together with the introduction of napalm, were effective and frightening enough. Bombing surveys of Hiroshima undertaken shortly after the war had ended concluded as much.


The Survey has estimated that the damage and casualties caused at Hiroshima by the one atomic bomb dropped from a single plane would have required 220 B-29s carrying 1,200 tons of incendiary bombs, 400 tons of high-explosive bombs, and 500 tons of anti-personnel fragmentation bombs, if conventional weapons, rather than an atomic bomb, had been used.

— D’Olier, Franklin, ed. (1946). United States Strategic Bombing Survey, Summary Report (Pacific War), p. 24


They could have completely destroyed a city the size of Hiroshima ever other day with conventional weapons alone (as they did), and at much less expense, whereas it would have taken several months to build more atomic bombs. It wasn’t the atomic bomb that forced Japan’s surrender. As Hasegawa has clearly shown, what finally forced Japan’s surrender was the Soviet Union’s entry into the war. The fear of a Soviet invasion was the main motivating factor. The threat of more atomic bombs no doubt compounded that fear, but the bomb didn’t end the war, it only started a new one. The use of the atomic bomb on Japan was a live target demonstration, serving as the opening salvo of the Cold War – two of them, the second to show it could be done again and again – but for Russia (and others) not Japan. There was no tactical advantage to be gained from the destruction of Hiroshima or Nagasaki, particularly at such a late stage in the war when they knew Japan was soon about to capitulate. The use of the atomic bomb on Japan was purely strategic. It kept Stalin out of the Tsugaru Strait and prevented another Berlin.

Be that as it may, I still think they should have spared Japan and dropped it directly on Stalin – and Truman/Byrnes.

Some would argue that Japan deserved it, especially for what they did in China. So why care? What do you expect when the dehumanising effects of total war, mindless vengeance and fortuitous expediency combine? Total war is not a very forgiving business. And even if the Japanese didn’t exactly deserve it, they at least brought it upon themselves. 33 million people is a lot of people. 200,000 atomic bomb fatalities doesn’t even begin to compare. And we must remember that total war does mean total, women and children too. Some would even argue that in total war there is no difference between civilians and soldiers; that that’s what total war does: it involves us all, totally – and dehumanises us all to the same extent. That’s when it becomes all about the numbers. Consider the Battle of Okinawa, for instance. 110,000 Japanese soldiers and Okinawan conscripts. An almost 100% casualty rate. They stood against nearly 800,000 U.S. troops. Total U.S. casualties: around 80,000. An expeditionary casualty rate of anything over 10% is unsustainable, both in practical battlefield and U.S. domestic political terms. That’s where the Russians come in. The U.S. will throw plenty of money at a problem but they’re not willing to throw away too many of their “boys”. Russia, on the other hand, doesn’t have any money, so they’ll happily throw away most of their “boys” – millions of them, if need be. One of our “boys” is worth at least 46 of theirs. Russia threw away nearly 23 million of them during World War II, the U.S. around 500,000. But then Stalin never had to worry about getting reelected. That’s where the poison gas comes in. The U.S. military had a nice big healthy stockpile of it in Australia. They estimated over five million Japanese casualties once chemical weapons were authorised for use on the battlefield. The Imperial Japanese forces had already been using them in China. Why then should they not be used in Japan? As soon as U.S. casualties started reaching unsustainable numbers they would have resorted to gas. Total war is total. And let’s not forget Shiro Ishii and Unit 731, Pearl Harbour, the Nanjing Massacre, a 27% prisoner of war fatality rate, suicide bombers, child soldiers, etc. Also remember that chemical weapons would have killed a lot more people than any atomic bomb could have at the time. One could even argue that the atomic bomb spared Australia from being complicit in any of that.

To this I would counter that this is not a question of deserving it or not. My question is not a moral one. I’m simply asking if the use of the atomic bomb on Japan was tactically required to force surrender. The answer is no, it was not. In that case, should we not then question if it was a war crime? I’m no legal expert, but I’m pretty sure wiping out whole populations, women and children, is in direct contravention of at least one or more articles of the Geneva Convention. Regardless of whether Japan deserved it or not, isn’t a war crime still a crime?

As a side note, Shiro Ishii, director of Unit 731 and responsible for the deaths of an estimated 300,000 people, was never prosecuted after the war. He received an “honorary” degree from Harvard University and was then promptly recruited into the U.S. biological and chemical weapons/pharmaceutical industry. So don’t even talk to me about deserving it. Why did so many Japanese people have to suffer for the sins of a privileged few, many of whom were never punished for their crimes after the war? What would they know about deserving it? Why collectively punish the victims while awarding the perpetrators honorary Harvard degrees? Why dehumanise all Japanese people even further than the Imperial Japanese Forces already had? Can anyone ever be deserving of that?

Likewise, this whole “all war is immoral, total war even more so” argument also fails to convince. It didn’t convince John Hersey and it sure doesn’t convince me. If all war is immoral (a euphemism for evil), and total war even more so, should it not be considered a crime to wage war? I know what I think. War in itself is a crime, in and of itself. No question about it. So with that question answered, the original question then becomes: Who is ever deserving of having a war crime perpetrated against them? I think we all know. I didn’t really need to read Hersey or visit Hiroshima to know the answer to that question. I mean it’s a natural enough conclusion to draw on one’s own and doesn’t really require any further research. In any case, it must be said that nowhere in Hiroshima does Hersey even attempt to answer that question (directly). In the end, he hands the crux of the matter over to the old German Catholic priest and closes with a ten-year-old boy on a bridge.


“The crux of the matter is whether total war in its present form is justifiable, even when it serves a just purpose. Does it not have material and spiritual evil as its consequences which far exceed whatever good might result? When will our moralists give us a clear answer to this question?”

A voice from above: Indeed, when?

Beyond what I’ve already written, I’m not going to attempt to answer that question either. I’m not a moralist. I’m not even a journalist. All I know is I agree with Miss Toshiko Sasaki, a clerk in the personnel department of the East Asia Tin Works. Sasaki-san was seriously maimed on the day of the bombing. She never married or had children and tragically converted to Catholicism a few years later. She then became a nun. So, just like Hersey, I too shall leave the moral question unanswered, for the most part, and I too shall end my time in Hiroshima on a bridge – but not before handing the crux of the matter over to Miss Toshiko Sasaki. As I’m sure we’d all agree, Sasaki-san felt that more notice should be given to the causes than to the instruments of total war.


Her rather bitter opinion was that it was the more lightly affected hibakusha and power-hungry politicians who focussed on the A-bomb, and that not enough thought was given to the fact that warfare had indiscriminately made victims of Japanese who had suffered atomic and incendiary bombings, Chinese civilians who had been attacked by the Japanese, reluctant young Japanese and American soldiers who were drafted to be killed or maimed, and, yes, Japanese prostitutes and their mixed-blood babies.

After my visit to the Hiroshima Peace Memorial Museum, I walked all the way back to my hotel, in the rain. I crossed the Ōta River. Very low river level. I could see all the way to the bottom. I stood on the Aioi Bridge for a while and looked over the side. As I turned to walk away, a flash of silver! A gigantic silver carp, or ginbuna (Carassius langsdorfii), swimming slowly up river. It’s different seeing them in their native environment. Majestic.


*

Back in Tokyo. I took a stroll through Sensō-ji today – packed with Korean tourists, and two German backpackers by way of Patagonia. How do they carry around all that bulky kit? And why? Das ist ein fucking Tempel! Then I went clothes shopping again. I bought some more Uniqlo t-shirts and another pair of GU black pants. And I already told you how much, so don’t bother asking. I also took a walk around Shibuya (a slightly less annoying non-Venetion Japanese version of Times Square), then took a walk through Tokyo Metro Park. No birdsong, except for crows and pigeons. No cherry blossoms either, and only three homeless cunts – four, including me.

Some stupid old homeless cunt (me) walks into a bar in Roppongi Hills. It’s one of those fancy “midtown” bars where you’d only go if you were forced to: for “business”. I was only there to hear Sarah sing – and because it was the only bar I could find, and I’d walked quite a long way that day and I was very thirsty and I really did feel like drinking a beer. I usually don’t drink beer, I swear. None of the staff there were wearing masks, nor were any of the patrons. I forgot I was wearing a mask. I approached the bar and requested an Asahi (made in China). The bartender, a dapper young gentleman, wearing a black single-breasted slim-fit Armani business suit in natural stretch light wool, did not appear to understand me. Again I requested an Asahi. Oh, Ahhsaaheee! he corrected me. There were two young men sitting at the bar to my right – your traditional Japanese family business boys, I assumed. They both began giggling at my clumsy Japanese beer-ordering etiquette and mocked my inept pronunciation. Anyone who’s spent any time learning another language or speaking a non-native tongue knows what that feels like – and this was a well known brand name. My dapper young bartender, after fully satisfying himself he had corrected my monotonally unaccented pronunciation of that particular word sufficiently to his liking, then proceeded to pour my beer, with the aid of some kind of fancy-looking elongated ice-cream scoop, the first time I’ve ever seen a beer poured that way. There was an older gentleman seated alone at the bar to my left. He was drinking red wine from a ridiculously large wine glass. He cast a disapproving eye over his younger countrymen, as they continued to discuss amongst themselves, at volume and at length, this newly arrived stupid old homeless cunt’s unforgivable mispronunciation of that most hallowed of Japanese words, Ahhsaaheee! He graciously offered me the bar stool immediately to his right, which I respectfully declined. After about three and half minutes of watching my dapper young bartender perform his elaborate beer-ice-cream scooping and pouring ritual, and after carefully explaining to him that I wished to pay for my Ahhsaaheee! immediately (I only had about ten minutes to spare), I then went and sat at a table outside by myself and drank my Ahhsaaheee! in silence. Just before my long-awaited Ahhsaaheee! was eventually poured and served, the two young gentlemen to my right paid their bill and exited the bar. As they passed behind me, one of them deliberately elbowed me wimpishly in the back. The other one called me unagi. I think that’s the first time I’ve ever been mistaken for a New Zealander (that I know of). It felt kind of good. Unagi probably means something rather unflattering when used in that context. Like I care. I am unagi! Twenty years ago I would have severely reprimanded them (even six years ago), but nothing was going to spoil my evening that evening. I had come to Roppongi Hills to hear Sarah sing. And so I did.

A voice from below: How much you pay for Roppongi Hills Ahhsaaheee! (made in China)?

As I was saying, I had come to Roppongi Hills to hear Sarah sing. She told me I must believe in Spring. How could I not? The venue was atop a towering steel-glass corporate facade full of every overpriced designer label you’d care not to think of: Gucci, Louis Vuitton, Dolce & Gabbana, Bottega Veneta, Douchei Figenarso, you name it. I felt somewhat out of place as I walked past them all and up the escalators. Thankfully, they used very low-level lighting inside the venue itself, so no one could tell I was wearing Uniqlo and GU. A handsome young Japanese gentleman with shinning eyes of sapphire blue guided me to my table, after first guiding me to what he soon determined to be the wrong table (far right of stage right). I think Mister Sapphire Eyes was sent in to case me out, as they say. He spoke unusually fluent English. He asked me where I was from. I told him New Zealand. He then told me they were having trouble sending me something in the mail. I’m not sure what he meant by that, but I enquired no further. Who is “they”? And what exactly are “they” trying to send me? A replacement JRail pass perhaps? Or yet another rejection letter? He said he studied at the University of Otago. I asked him which campus. He made no reply. I think he put me at the journalist’s table (front left of stage left). There were three other people seated at the table and they all had that lonely sweaty journalist kind of vibe about them. I think I missed a few important messages during the interim (this isn’t my day job). I did, however, finish writing the base structure as I was eating my ultra-processed all-meat Italian meal and waiting for Sarah to go on stage. The woman seated opposite me looked very familiar, and very Italian. I wanted very much to speak with her and find out who she was. But they still had those thick transparent plastic facial barricade things up everywhere and I was way too preoccupied with Sarah.

She wore a simple black dress, but over it she wore a translucent slip of silver that lit up the entire stage. She was more beautiful than life itself.

A voice from above: You must believe in Spring!

I ordered the Cherry Blossom Girl drink. Vodka and tonic with cherry-flavoured syrup. How could I not? Sarah ordered one too. How could she not? She toasted the crowd and took a sip. I’m not sure exactly what she said; she spoke in Japanese, for the most part. I think it had something to do with believing in yourself before you can believe in Spring, or something of that nature. As she turned from the audience to set her drink down, she made the same face I made when I first tasted mine.

A voice from below: You don’t believe in Spring, and she doesn’t believe in you. All writers are liars.

Good thing I’m nothing but a whore then.

A voice from above: You’re also a stupid old homeless cunt.

That may be true also, but I’m only here to hear Sarah sing. So what does it matter?

A voice from below: Yeah, right.

She sang Comme d’habitude and Calling You.


Tout seul j’irai me coucher
Dans ce grand lit froid
Comme d’habitude
Mes larmes je les cacherai
Comme d’habitude

Hot dry wind blows right through me
Baby’s crying and I can’t sleep
But we both know a change is coming
It’s coming closer...


I don’t know if those were real tears Sarah shed while she sang Comme d’habitude, but I do know my tears were real when she sang Calling You. It’s a good thing she only sang one song like that, otherwise I think I would have broken down completely in an endless downpour of torrential tears. Baby’s crying indeed. I knew she knew exactly what she was doing to me by singing that song. I tried not to look at her, for fear of becoming totally overwhelmed. Occasionally, she would glide across the stage, however, and the lights would reflect a flash of silver from her dress that would instinctively catch my eye. It happened maybe three or four times. I couldn’t help but look up at her during those moments, as the glare of the stage lights refracted through the watery lenses of mine eyeballs and stars appeared to grow out of the air. But for the most part I kept my eyes down. I’m pathetic, I know.

A voice from above: She’s too perfect to be singing Disney songs in Roppongi Hills. Seize her now and take her away with you!

And then she sang some Disney songs. No offence intended, and especially not to Sarah, but fuck Bambi dead in the arse with a great big plastic penis. Die, Bambi! And good riddance, I say. We deliberately run those things down in Aotearoa, and in Australia too.

A voice from above: Soon the only animals left on this planet will be cutesiepoo Disneyesque cartoon caricatures of them.

Die, Bambi!

Before she began, she recited a poem, in English, about Spring. But I don’t remember anything about that. It wasn’t very good. Then she picked up her violin and played both parts of the bird song bit from Vivaldi’s Le quattro stagioni, la primavera, looped them over one another, with a deliberate sour note at the end, and then added the Little April Shower song from Disney’s Bambi on top of it.

Die, Bambi!

And then she overlaid another song – some funky Disney dance number I’m not familiar with – and started improvising on keyboards to that. That’s when all the older Japanese gentlemen stood up and started clapping along and dancing wildly. She had them all in the palm of her hand. It was a veritable utagaki of rampant J-Disneyism!

How could they not believe in Spring?

But then it must be difficult for anyone not to believe in Spring, which is now becoming Summer, these days. For if, as is expected, Spring becomes Summer in future – if there are only two seasons, like in tropical regions – then there won’t be any cherry trees. It’ll be Summer all year round, with only a wet and a dry season. 47% of the world’s plant species are at risk of extinction. But I suppose I’ll go on pretending I believe in Spring for as long as I can. I must apparently.

In any event, I shouldn’t let any politically motivated environmental Disney issues cloud my perception. Sarah already knows how I feel about Bambi. And I probably shouldn’t even have mentioned what she was wearing, or drinking. Her voice was perfect and so was she.

And the other three musicians were pretty good too (except the piano player). They weren’t as good as me though. But then Sarah always does seem to prefer the hairy armed variety, which makes perfect sense, and I’m not a musician.

If she had any sense about her she would never want or need anything from me, in any sense. She’s a princess, I am unagi. She lives in the light, I live in the shadows. She still believes in Spring, I do not. I am unagi! Oh well, I guess it’s back to writing shitty little love songs about Ben Stiller and Tom Hanks.

I didn’t expect anything less than total rejection. She is a princess, after all, and she is an Australian too. No dumplings, soba or saké for me. Still, I thought she’d at least ask me out for tea. Not that it matters much. Sarah can’t save me. No one can. Now I guess I really will have to set myself on fire. Ben Stiller and Tom Hanks are totally fucked now.

A voice from above: So why are you still checking her social?

Primarily owing to repressed curiosity, I must admit I did take a look at her X-twatter account again today. The first post was a picture of her with some junior Tom Hanks-like actor type dude (the very same Mister Hairy Arms, I assume). He was pretending to play an electrified imitation guitar with a small piece of plastic. He had such an insipid and banal expressionless expression on his face. Sarah was playing her violin with her back to the camera. She was wearing that perfect yellow dress. I became so irrationally jealous. I wanted to punch him as hard as I could directly in his insipid and banal expressionless face. I couldn’t bear to look at any of her other posts. Or was that the intent?

A voice from below: Stop writing to her then. That’ll teach her.

I’ve stopped writing to her. I deactivated my fake X-twatter account (Jesus HFN Christ) and I unsubscribed from her YouBube channel too. I don’t think she wants me to write to her anymore, or ever did. And she probably doesn’t want me stalking her on social media either. I guess I can understand why. I’ll finish writing about My Three WHORES, and the other unfinished bits, then that’s it. This has to end at some point. I’m running out of time. We all are.

A voice from above: If you really loved her you’d sing her a non-shitty little love song till stars grew out of the air.

It wouldn’t make any difference. Besides, isn’t that kind of what I’m already doing?

A voice from below: How much you pay for shitty little love song?

In total? Including this $350 Uber ride? None of your fucking business. Just drive the fucking car.

As I was saying, I’m running out of time. So I’ll end by saying I didn’t feel empty and hopeless after the show, once I was no longer in Sarah’s presence. I felt calm. Then I thought about how I felt after leaving Kylie’s book launch that night in Perth. I compared how miserable I felt that night to how calm I felt then. Why so different? Then I began to understand why.

A voice from above: Because you don’t love her. You love...

Shh.

I didn’t do much for the rest of that evening. I bought some more instant coffee and a pint of milk (made in China) then went back to my hotel. I should have smuggled in some New Zealand milk as well. I fell asleep at about 1AM while watching Japanese commercial television. I didn’t dream. I awoke at 4AM, ears bleeding, to the overwrought squawking strains of that godawful deepfake Taylor Swift song, Never mind I’ll find someone like you. I wish nothing but the best for you too. Don’t forget me... A truly horrendous sound, both voice and verse. It caused intense physical pain in my ears, and in my brain. Quite the contrast from the previous evening, I thought.


*

I finally ate out at a restaurant today, a tonkatsu restaurant in Shimbashi Station. I had to make up for all those instant noodles somehow. Very good tonkatsu. Crispy on the outside yet tender and juicy within. But while eating it, as I sat there alone at the counter, alone together in silence with the other solitary diners, each of us hermetically encased in thick transparent plastic facial barricades, all I could think about was Sarah. I was thinking about something else she said during the show: that life is nothing but a monotonous load of meaningless shit with rare moments of ephemeral beauty in between. I’m paraphrasing, of course, but words to that effect. I don’t think I’ve ever felt more alone while thinking about that. I suppose that really is the best we can ever hope for. How depressing. But I didn’t cry. I think I would have if the tonkatsu weren’t so good, but I didn’t cry.

It’s illegal to smoke outside in Japan.

A voice from below: How much you pay for Shimbashi Station tonkatsu?

Shh.

The smoking room at Shimbashi Station, right beside that 1940s-era steam locomotive (C11 292) – It blows a whistle three times a day! – both young and old, male and female (predominately male, “salarymen”), 40 people in a small enclosed space designed for no more than ten (I think I smoked the equivalent of 20 cigarettes in about five minutes), eyes down always (smokers generally have better taste in footwear than non-smokers – just an observation), total silence but for the strained whirling of that solitary tar-gunked 1980s-era Daikin air-conditioning unit, all of us vainly trying to keep at least 0.15 metres of distance, cast adrift from the main of society, forever on the periphery, always searching for that ever-elusive well-ventilated smoking room with less than ten people.

I can’t believe I forgot to bring vape juice.

Japan needs to ban the sale of all big tobacco products and legalise the sale of no-plume odourless nicotine vapes.


*

I love travelling alone, especially in Japan and especially on trains. No plans, just go. Change my mind any time I want and go somewhere else. Get off the train here or there any time the mood strikes me. No discussion, no explanation, no fuss.
